Chem. Soc. Rev., 2013, 42, 6128-6185Near-infrared phosphorescence: materials and applicationsHaifeng Xiang*, Jinghui Cheng, Xiaofeng Ma, Xiangge Zhou and Jason Joseph ChrumaCollege of Chemistry, Sichuan University, Chengdu, 610041, China. E-mail: xianghaifeng@scu.edu.cn;  Fax: +86 28-85412291;   Tel: +86 28-85412290Received 28th January 2013First published on 7th May 2013AbstractRoom-temperature phosphorescent materials that emit light in the visible (red, green, and blue; from 400 to 700 nm) have been a major focus of research and development during the past decades, due to their applications in organic light-emitting diodes (OLEDs), light-emitting electrochemical cells, photovoltaic cells, chemical sensors, and bio-imaging. In recent years, near-infrared (NIR) phosphorescence beyond the visible region (700–2500 nm) has emerged as a new, promising, and challenging research field with potential applications toward NIR OLEDs, telecommunications, night vision-readable displays. Moreover, NIR phosphorescence holds promise for in vivo imaging, because cells and tissues exhibit little absorption and auto-fluorescence in this spectral region. This review describes the overall progress made in the past ten years on NIR phosphorescent transition-metal complexes including Cu(I), Cu(II), Cr(III), Re(I), Re(III), Ru(II), Os(II), Ir(III), Pt(II), Pd(II), Au(I), and Au(III) complexes, with a primary focus on material design complemented with a selection of optical, electronic, sensory, and biologic applications. A critical comparison of various NIR phosphorescent materials reported in the literature and a blueprint for future development in this field are also provided.Haifeng XiangHaifeng Xiang obtained his PhD from the Department of Chemistry and Department of Electrical & Electronic Engineering, The University of Hong Kong, Hong Kong, P. His research interests include the development of natural product-based electrophiles as fluorescent linchpin reagents in bioconjugate synthesis.1 IntroductionInfrared (IR) light is electromagnetic radiation with longer wavelengths than those of visible light (400–700 nm), extending from the nominal red edge of the visible spectrum at 700 nm to 1 mm. This range of wavelengths corresponding to a frequency range of approximately 430 THz to 300 GHz can be divided into three parts: near-infrared (NIR), mid-infrared, and far-infrared regions. The NIR region typically falls in the spectrum from 700 to 2500 nm (120 to 430 THz). Nearly 50% of the energy from the Sun reaching the Earth is in the form of NIR radiation, as shown in Fig. 1a. At present, one of the key limiting factors for organic solar cells or photovoltaic cells (PVCs) is the mismatch between the absorption spectrum of organic dyes and the solar emission spectrum, thus it is important to extend the absorption of organic dyes from the ultra violet (UV)/visible into the NIR region. Using NIR light for sensing and detection offers additional benefits for being invisible and having less interference. Moreover, biological tissues have minimal absorption and auto-fluorescence in the NIR region (Fig. 1b), allowing for deeper penetration of light in comparison to visible light. This “imaging window” is a clear advantage for the use of NIR materials as tags or stains for real-time in vivo biological imaging. NIR light is also commonly used in fiber optic telecommunication and night vision-readable displays. Therefore, considerable and growing attention has been spent during the past decade on the development of NIR light-emitting materials or dyes for a variety of applications in the chemical and life sciences.1–15 A search in Web of Knowledge, Thomson Reuters, for documents containing the terms “NIR/IR” and “emission” or its synonym in the title and published from 2000 to September, 2012 resulted in about 8400 hits (Fig. 2), in which ∼30% are about NIR light-emitting materials. Fig. 1  (a) The Sun's power spectrum (AM1.5) reaching the Earth. (b) Absorbance of various tissue and blood components from 200 nm to 10 μm (adapted from ref. 14).  Fig. 2  Published documents, searched in Web of Knowledge, Thomson Reuters, from 2000 to September, 2012, about NIR/IR light-emitting materials. In general, visible16–31 and NIR1–15 light-emitting materials consist of inorganic materials (metal oxides and semiconductor nanocrystals) and organic materials (metal complexes and pure organic compounds), which are fluorescent and widely studied due to their rich chemical structures, ease of chemical modification and high fluorescence quantum yield (ΦF). In contrast to the short emission decay lifetime (τ) associated with standard fluorescence, lanthanide complexes32–36 typically exhibit long-lived emissions, in the order of microseconds to milliseconds, in the visible and NIR regions. Accordingly, appropriate delay time filters allow for the avoidance of interference from short-lived background fluorescence and scattering light with these complexes. However, the extremely sharp metal-centered (MC) emission of most lanthanide complexes is not phosphorescence in the conventional sense because it does not originate from a transition between two states of different multiplicity and is not sensitive to oxygen.32,37,38 Recently, room-temperature (RT) visible (red, green, and blue) phosphorescent d6, d8, and d10 transition-metal complexes,37–59 such as Ru(II),60–64 Pt(II),65–69 Ir(III),70,71 Au(I), Au(III),72–75 and Cu(I),76 have been a major focus of research, due to their wide and useful applications in phosphorescent organic light-emitting diodes (OLEDs),37,42–44,47,49,54,56,57,62,64,68,69,71 light-emitting electrochemical cells (LECs),40,42,46,55,63 triplet–triplet annihilation based upconversion (TTAU),51,59 PVCs,41,47,53,61 chemosensors,38–39,52,57,58 therapy,45,74 and bio-imaging.48,58 Compared with traditional fluorescent materials, phosphorescent transition-metal complexes offer several important advantages. Phosphorescence originates in the transition from the triplet excited state (T1) to the singlet ground state (S0), and thus enables the harvest of both singlet and triplet excitons to achieve a maximum internal quantum efficiency of 100% in OLEDs.77,78 Phosphorescence provides a longer decay lifetime up to milliseconds. Phosphorescence exhibits larger Stokes' shifts compared to fluorescence due to the energy loss in intersystem crossing between S1 and T1, thus decreasing spectral interference between the excitation light and the phosphorescent emission. The possible excited states of phosphorescent transition-metal complexes52 include triplet metal-to-ligand charge transfer (3MLCT), intraligand charge transfer (3ILCT), ligand-to-ligand charge transfer (3LLCT), ligand-to-metal charge transfer (3LMCT), metal–metal-to-ligand charge transfer (3MMLCT), ligand-to-metal–metal charge transfer (3LMMCT), and metal-to-ligand–ligand charge transfer (3MLLCT), which depend on the metal centers, chemical structures and triplet state energy levels of the ligands, local environment, and intermolecular interaction, and thus their absorption (λabs) and emission wavelength (λem), τ, and phosphorescence quantum efficiency (ΦP) can be fine-tuned through the modification of ligand structures and metal centres.Up to now, there have been some published reviews summarizing the recent developments and applications of NIR fluorescence1–15 and visible phosphorescence.37–76 Combining their advantages, NIR phosphorescence has emerged as a new, promising, and challenging research field. This critical review, which focuses mainly on material design, describes the overall progress made in the last ten years in NIR phosphorescent transition-metal complexes and their applications. Additionally, a blueprint for future development in this field is suggested. In this review, NIR emitters are defined as materials where their λem is equal to or longer than 700 nm at RT, but sometimes some of their derivatives, whose λem is shorter than 700 nm but longer than 665 nm are also involved for comparative purposes.2. General principles of NIR phosphorescence2.1 Nature of the excited state in phosphorescencePhotoluminescence (PL) that includes both fluorescence (F) and phosphorescence (P) is spontaneous emission following absorption of electromagnetic radiation of an appropriate frequency in the UV/visible region. Phosphorescence, a spin-forbidden transition, occurs during relaxation of an electron from the triplet excited state to the ground state (T1 → S0), while fluorescence is a radiative transition between electronic states of the same multiplicity (S1 → S0). Since phosphorescence involves the intersystem crossing between S1 and T1, τ (10−6–10−3 s) and Stokes' shift of phosphorescence are typically longer and larger than that of fluorescence (τ: 10−9–10−7 s), as shown in Fig. 3. Since the ground state of oxygen is a triplet state, it can quench phosphorescence by through-space energy transfer with the long-lived triplet of the luminophore. Accordingly, another important feature of phosphorescent materials is that they can serve as oxygen sensors.38,79–83 Fig. 3  Jablonski diagram: E - energy, A - absorption, F - fluorescence, P - phosphorescence, IC - internal conversion, ISC - intersystem crossing, VR - vibrational relaxation, S - singlet state, T - triplet state. It is rare to observe RT phosphorescence from pure organic compounds due to the predominance of accessible thermal decay pathways. Therefore, incorporation into typically organized media (in the host of cyclodextrin,84 polymer,85,86 or crystal87–89) or the addition of heavy atoms such as halogens,88–91 Tl(I),92 Pb(II),92 P(III),93 Sb(III),93 or Bi(III)92–94 [McClure heavy-atom spin-orbit coupling (SOC) effect]95 are required for RT phosphorescence. Still, most of these materials exhibit weak phosphorescence in solution at RT. A more efficient way to achieve RT phosphorescence is adopting cyclometalated transition-metal complexes. These complexes exhibit higher stability and stronger RT phosphorescence, in both solution and solid state, than other organic dyes because of the following three reasons. (1) Cyclometalated transition-metal complexes tend to form a strong bonding interaction with transition-metal elements. (2) The stronger metal–ligand bonding increases the d–d energy gap and affords less radiation-less quenching due to the suppressed population of the higher lying, repulsive d–d excited state; this is particularly true for the third-row transition-metal elements. (3) The occurrence of close-lying, ligand-centered 3π–π* electronic transitions allows for facile tuning of the emission wavelength.49,96For transition-metal complexes, the construction of Jablonski diagrams via electronic configurations from the molecular orbitals (MOs) description follows the same general lines described for organic molecules.97 Since most NIR transition-metal complexes are Pt(II) complexes, a schematic MO diagram for Pt(II) complexes is shown in Fig. 4 as an example. Unlike other well-known octahedral d6 Ru(II), Ir(III), Re(I), or Os(II) complexes, d8 Pt(II) complexes adopt a square-planar geometry and possess a vacant coordination site at the Pt(II) center, which allows axial substrate-binding interactions and might lead to effective emission quenching (Fig. 5). Therefore, Pt(II) complexes that have diverse excited states including not only (π–π*) 3ILCT and (d–π*) 3MLCT common to normal transition-metal complexes, but also 3MMLCT can emit strong phosphorescence from the visible to the NIR region resulting from avoiding the quenching of free axial coordinating site and strong Pt–Pt interactions. Fig. 4  Schematic energy level diagram of MOs and possible electronic transitions in a square-planar Pt(II) complex. 1: π–π*, 2: LMCT, 3: ligand field transition (LF or d–d), 4: MLCT, 5: d–s MC Transition, 6: d–p MC Transition.  Fig. 5  Schematic geometry diagram of d6 Ru(II) and d8 Pt(II) complexes. 2.1.1 MC d–d excited state. Typical metal complexes with a partially filled d shell at the metal center are characterized by low-energy MC excitation states which arise from electron hopping between the nonbonding (dπ) and antibonding (dσ*) orbitals. These d–d transitions are Laporte-forbidden, showing exceedingly low transition probability for the occurrence of both MC-based absorption and emission signals. The emissions derived from the d–d excited states of Pt(II) complexes are broad, structure-less and relatively matrix-independent but temperature dependent. The quantum yield is generally low, but can be increased by removing high-frequency vibrations from the coordination sphere; a typical example is [Pt(bpy)(Cl)2]98–101 (bpy = 2,2′-bipyridine) (Fig. 6). The emitting state of [Pt(bpy)(Cl)2] was assigned to a 3(d–d) transition as early as in 1989, but a more probable 3MLCT assignment cannot be excluded.98 The 3(d–d) emission from this complex in diluted solution is vanishingly weak but at 77 K, a broad unstructured emission with λem at ca. 620 nm is observed in diluted glassy solution. Another milestone example that exhibits green emission (λem = 514 nm and τ = 9 μs) in aqueous solution is [Pt2(μ-P2O5H2)4]4−.102 This pyrophosphito-bridged diplatinum(II) complex with strong Pt–Pt interaction exhibits unique photophysical properties from a 3[ndσ* → (n + 1)pσ] excited state (Fig. 7). Fig. 6  Chemical structure of some phosphorescent transition-metal complexes with different excitation states.  Fig. 7  Qualitative molecular orbital interaction diagram for diplatinum(II) complex and the relative energy between LLCT, MMLCT and MC 3[dσ*–pσ] states. 2.1.2 ILCT. Intraligand (IL) or ligand-centered (LC) excited states originate from electronic transitions between π orbitals that are mainly localized on the ligand chromophore. As such, if the metal perturbation upon coordination is minimized, the luminescent complex may exhibit a transition that has relatively solvent-independent spectral properties closely resembling the free ligand states. The 3LC emission energy is much less affected by heavy metal ions. A typical example is [Pt(TPP)] (TPP = meso-tetraphenylporphyrin) (Fig. 6). The 3LC emission from this complex in CH2Cl2 solution at RT occurs at λem = 660 nm, similar with the free ligand of TPP (λem = 650 nm) (Fig. 8). Another typical example is [Pt(bpy)(en)]2+ (en = ethanediamine).98 The 3LC emission from this complex in diluted glassy solution at 77 K occurs at λem = 485 nm with overlapping vibronic progressions at ca. 1400 and 1000 cm−1, which is in excellent agreement with the phosphorescence spectrum of [Zn(bpy)2]2+. Fig. 8  Emission spectra of [Pt(TPP)] and TPP in CH2Cl2 at RT. 2.1.3 MLCT. The photochemistry of transition-metal complexes is mainly dominated by studies of the 3MLCT excited state of luminescent Ru(II) polypyridine complexes,103 such as [Ru(bpy)3]2+ (Fig. 5 and 6). 3MLCT states involve electronic transitions from a metal-based d orbital to a ligand-based delocalized π* antibonding orbital. In a conventional sense, the corresponding excitation results in metal oxidation and concomitant ligand reduction. These transitions are commonly observed in the middle- and late-transition-metal complexes possessing relatively low oxidation potentials.44 As a result, the emission energy of 3MLCT, which depends on the reduction potential of the ligand and the oxidation potential of the metal ion, can be tuned by the auxiliary ligands and affected by the polarity of the solvent/medium. A typical example of 3MLCT Pt(II) complexes is [Pt(phbpy)(PPh3)]+ (phbpy = 6-phenyl-2,2′-bipyridine and PPh3 = triphenylphosphine)104 (Fig. 6), which exhibits an intense greenish yellow emission in degassed MeCN solution at 298 K (λem = 535 nm and τ = 0.9 μs). Other typical examples include [Pt(3,3(MeO2C)2bpy)Cl2]100 [3,3(MeO2C)2bpy = 3,3′-dicarbethoxy-2,2′-bipyridine] and [Pt(5,5Me2bpy)(CCPh)2]105 (5,5Me2bpy = 5,5′-dimethyl-2,2′-bipyridine and CCPh = phenylacetylene) (Fig. 6). The lowest-lying excited state of [Pt(3,3(MeO2C)2bpy)(Cl)2] is now the 3MLCT state, but the d–d state lies only a little higher in energy, offering the 3MLCT a non-radiative decay pathway and also rendering this complex non-emissive at RT like [Pt(bpy)(Cl)2]. On the other hand, [Pt(5,5Me2bpy)(CCPh)2] is much more emissive at RT than [Pt(bpy)(Cl)2] both in fluid solution (λmax = 545 nm, τ=1.6 μs, and ΦP = 0.64 in CH2Cl2) and in the solid state, due to the stronger ligand-field associated with the acetylide compared to the chloride co-ligands. Notably, many highly emissive transition-metal complexes, especially for high-performance phosphorescent OLEDs applications, are Ir(III) or Pt(II) complex, such as [Ir(ppy)3]106 (ppy = 2-phenylpyridyl), [Ir(ppy)2(acac)] (acac = acetyl acetone),107 and [Pt(ppy)(acac)],108 with emission characteristic of phosphorescence from 3MLCT and/or 3ILCT.2.1.4 LMCT and LLCT. Transition-metal complexes with metal atoms in high oxidation states can show luminescence from 3LMCT.44 These complexes typically constitute early transition-metal complexes, such as Ta(V) complexes,109,110 and exhibit RT phosphorescence at the lower energy region of the visible spectra with lower quantum efficiencies. Other candidates that exhibit 3LMCT emission are polynuclear d10 complexes involving Au(I) and Cu(I) complexes.393LMCT excited states are seldom the lowest in energy for Pt(II) complexes. In heteroleptic complexes where LL′CT excited states have been proposed, it is likely that the LL′CT excited states should also exhibit ML′CT character. This is because Pt(5d) orbitals should strongly couple with the π orbitals of L′. Typical examples are [Pt(α-diimine)(dithiolate)] complexes,111,112 such as [Pt(dbbpy)(tdt)] (dbbpy = 4,4′-di-tert-butyl-2,2′-bipyridine and tdt = toluene-3,4-dithiolate) complex (Fig. 6), where L is dithiolate and L′ is the aromatic α-diimine ligand. Most of these complexes, however, have lower ΦP than previously mentioned for 3MLCT and/or 3ILCT emitting materials.2.1.5 MMLCT. Miskowski and co-workers proposed oligomeric 3MMLCT (Fig. 7) based on a series of discrete d8–d8 binuclear Pt(II) complexes, namely, [(Pt(tpy))2(μ-L)]n+ (tpy = 2,2′:6′,2′′-terpyridine, L = bidentate ligand),113,114 which exhibit red-to-NIR emission (λem = 630–730 nm), depending on the Pt–Pt distances (3.00–3.43 Å), in the solid state at RT. For shorter Pt–Pt contacts (3.00 Å) in [(Pt(tpy))2(μ-can)]3+ (can = arginine) (Fig. 6), the lowest excited state is 3[dσ*(dz2(Pt)) → π*(diimine)] (3MMLCT) (λem = 730 nm). As the intra platinum distance increases to 3.43 Å in [(Pt(tpy))2(μ-pz)]3+ (pz = pyrazol), the energy of 3[dσ*(dz2(Pt)) → π*(diimine)] also increases (λem = 630 nm). At sufficiently large Pt–Pt separations, the 3[dσ*(dz2(Pt)) → π*(diimine)] will move above 3[π → π*(diimine)]. For example, [(Pt(tpy))2(μ-pz)]3+ displays both 3[dσ*(dz2(Pt)) → π*(diimine)] and 3[π → π*(diimine)] emission.How to identify these excited states? Many methods, such as excited-state lifetime measurements,115,116 time-resolved luminescence spectroscopy,100,115,117,118 and computational investigations,44,119–121 have been used to understand the nature of the excited state in transition-metal complexes. The most important and useful transitions of phosphorescent transition-metal complexes are 3ILCT and 3MLCT. Yersin et al. utilized the amount of zero-field splitting (ZFS) to classify the degree of MLCT perturbation and SOC efficiency in the emitting triplet state for phosphorescent transition-metal complexes,56,122–126 thus providing a useful scale not only for an assessment of the MLCT perturbation in the T1 state but also for phosphorescence τ (Fig. 9). SOC provides a softening of the spin-forbidden character of the transitions between T1 and S0 resulting in a desired reduction of τ. Therefore, phosphorescent transition-metal complexes with larger ZFS are expected to have shorter τ values. In addition to ΦP and λem, τ is another important parameter of phosphorescence in regards to applications. For example, phosphorescent transition-metal complexes with short τ values (τ < 10 μs, ZFS > 10 cm−1, 3MLCT or 3MLCT/3ILCT) have less triplet–triplet annihilation and result in less roll-off in the quantum efficiency in phosphorescent OLEDs.127 On the other hand, due to the lack of direct incorporation with metal-center electron density, transition-metal complexes with 3ILCT are subject to much longer τ. This is useful for avoiding the spectral interference from short-lived background fluorescence and scattering light and it has clear advantages in such applications as chemical sensors and bio-imaging. It should be noted that mixing of 3ILCT and 3MLCT states may play an important role for decent ΦP, because this intersystem crossing can be enhanced for the systems involving notable MLCT participation.44 Fig. 9  Phosphorescence τ (300 K) versus ZFS (red curve) and ordering scheme showing metal complexes with increasing MLCT character in the emissive triplet state for transition-metal complexes (adapted from ref. 56 and 126). 2.2 Tuning the lowest excited statesIt is important to tune the lowest excited states, the type or energy (emission color or wavelength), of transition-metal complexes because they decide the photophysical properties. The most effective methods for tuning the lowest excited state involves modifications in the participating (cyclometalated and/or ancillary) ligands, such as changing the degree of π-conjugation, altering substituents, and adding heterocycles.44,128As mentioned previously, three Pt(II) bipyridine100 complexes (Fig. 10) with similar chemical structures exhibit different lowest excited states. The energy of 3ILCT [π–π*(bpy)] in [Pt(bpy)(en)]2+ is lower than that of 3MLCT [d–π*(bpy)] and 3LF (d–d) and thus the lowest excited state of [Pt(bpy)(en)]2+ is 3ILCT (Fig. 10). The large stabilization of 3MLCT and 3LF for [Pt(bpy)(Cl)2] can be attributed to the strongly σ-donating character of the chloride ligands, resulting in 3LF becoming the lowest excited state. Furthermore, the strongly electron-withdrawing substituents of –CO2Me on the bipyridine ligand of [Pt(3,3′-(MeO2C)2bpy)(Cl)2] have stabilized a bpy π* level, yielding a 3MLCT [d–π*(bpy)] state as the lowest energy excited state. In addition to cyclometalated ligands, ancillary ligands [–(CCR)] in [Pt(phbpy)(CCR)] heteroleptic complexes129 also exhibit a strong effect on the nature of the lowest excited state, as shown in Fig. 11. Fig. 10  Estimates of the energies of lowest excited states of three Pt(II) bpy complexes (adapted from ref. 100).  Fig. 11  The lowest excited states of [Pt(phbpy)(CCR)] complexes (adapted from ref. 129). 2.3 Tuning emission into NIR regionThe optical and electronic properties of organic molecules are determined by their energy gap, which is the energy separation between the highest occupied molecular orbital (HOMO) and lowest unoccupied molecular orbital (LUMO), also called the HOMO–LUMO gap. The red shift in the absorption and emission spectra resulting from a narrowing of this energy gap is a key in the design of NIR emitters. Generally, for NIR fluorescent materials, several design factors, such as the π-conjugation length, bond length alternation, and donor–acceptor (DA) system,3,5,11 should be considered. These factors are also useful for the ligand design in NIR phosphorescent transition-metal complexes. Moreover, the great success in the material design for visible phosphorescent transition-metal complexes, especially for phosphorescent OLEDs, provides useful experiences for extending visible emission into NIR emission.2.3.1 Effect of substituents in ligand. The effect of substituents on the emission characteristics is quite complex and generalizations should be made with caution. Both the nature and position of a substituent can alter these characteristics. As early as 1991, the first extended series of fac-tris-ppy Ir(III) complexes with different electron-withdrawing (e.g., –F, –CF3) and electron-donating (e.g., –t-Bu, –OMe) groups was reported by Watts and coworkers.130 Electron-withdrawing substituents tend to stabilize the HOMO by removing electron density from the metal and result in a blue shift of emission, whereas donating groups have an inverse effect. Moreover, the cyclometalated and ancillary ligands can be separately substituted with electron-withdrawing and -donating groups in heteroleptic complexes, which enables deliberate control of the excited state from blue to red emission,106–108,128,129 as shown in Fig. 12. Tuning phosphorescence from blue-green-red emission into the NIR region merely by the substituent effect, however, is rare, presumably since the contribution of ligand substituents to the emission characteristics is much smaller than that of the intrinsic π-conjugated system of the parent ligand. Some other effects, such as vibrational coupling of the excited and the ground state and energy gap law (see Section 2.4), must be considered as well. Fig. 12  Color versatility expressed by a series of six cationic Ir(III) complexes. The evolution of 3LC (vibrationally structured, high-energy bands) and 3MLCT character (structureless, low-energy bands) in the luminescence spectra are indicative of a mixed excited state (adapted from ref. 128). 2.3.2 Effect of π-conjugation length in the ligand. For fluorescent materials, the extension of the π-conjugated system can lead to a decrease in the energy gap to a certain extent.131–133 For example, by adjusting the number of fused naphthalene units in the rylenediimide dyes,131 the energy gap can be tuned over a quite large range, e.g., from roughly 1.29 eV (960 nm) to 2.15 eV (577 nm) (Fig. 13). However, the energy gap of a π-conjugated system can only be lowered to a certain limit by increasing the chain length. There is strong experimental support, in the case of polythiophene and phenyl–vinyl oligomers, that oligomers with more than 5 to 8 repeating units bear all of the essential electronic properties (e.g., absorption and emission of light) of infinite polymer chains.134–137 Another challenge is how to synthesize and improve the solubility of these rigid extended π-conjugated materials. Fig. 13  Absorption spectra of the entire tetraphenoxy-substituted rylenediimide series in CHCl3 (adapted from ref. 131). For phosphorescent transition-metal complexes, one of most effective ways to reduce the emission energy into NIR region involves expanding the length of π-conjugation in the ligand. There are many successful instances43,106–108,128,129 of red shifting in phosphorescent emission spectra by extending π-conjugation length in the ligand. For example, compared with [Ir(ppy)2(acac)], other [Ir(L)2(acac)] complexes with the larger π-conjugated system of either an isoquinoline or a naphthyl group, show a red shift in emission spectra (Fig. 14).43 The complex involved the rigid azaperylene ligand demonstrated a particularly marked red shift, emitting at 932 nm. This emission energy is very close to the phosphorescent emission from perylene (800 nm), suggesting that the excited state in this complexes is predominantly 3ILCT. Furthermore, if the ancillary ligand has a lower triplet energy than the cyclometalated moiety, this ligand becomes directly involved in the emission process. For example, the ancillary ligand of 1-ethynylpyrene in the [Pt(phbpy)(CC–pyrenyl-1)] heteroleptic complex (Fig. 11) affords a low-energy and narrow-bandwidth emission at 664 nm with major vibronic progression, τ and ΦP of 1230 cm−1, 20.8 μs and 0.01, respectively, in CH2Cl2 solution at 298 K. This deep-red emission is also assigned to 3ILCT in the ancillary ligand of 1-ethynylpyrene.129 Fig. 14  Ligand definitions and emission energies for a series of [Ir(L)2(acac)] complexes. Nonetheless, is it right that the more (the π-conjugated length in the ligand), the better (the decrease of energy gap)? On the one hand, the energy gap of a π-conjugated system for fluorescent materials or ligands can only be lowered to a certain value by increasing the number of π-bonds involved, as discussed previously. On the other hand, for phosphorescent transition-metal complexes, as the length of π-conjugation in the ligand increases, the extent of participation of metal d orbitals in the highest-occupied molecular orbitals decreases while the S1 → T1 energy gap increases, resulting in the attenuated ISC effect. As a result, the fluorescence becomes observable while the intensity of the phosphorescence decreases due to the combined effects of a lower yield of triplet formation, a decreased T1 → S0 radiative decay rate, and increased non-radiative decay as the excited state energy decreases.38 Thus, dual fluorescence/phosphorescence emission is obtained by reducing phosphorescence and increasing fluorescence. There are several examples138–142 of dual fluorescent/phosphorescent Pt(II) and Os(II) complexes, because these complexes usually have lower efficiency of spin-orbit coupling than Ir(III) complexes.140,142 For example, whereas [Pt(thpy)(acac)] [thpy = 2-(2-thienyl)pyridine] solely displays intense phosphorescence (ΦP = 0.36) from a triplet state of mixed 3π–π*/MLCT character, the phosphorescence of [Pt(th2py)(acac)] [th2py = 5-(2-pyridyl)-2,2′-bithiophene] is weak (ΦF+P = 5.8 × 10−3), strongly red shifted, and accompanied by higher-energy fluorescence (Fig. 15).140 Therefore, extending π-conjugated length in the ligand is a double-edged sword, in that it might have positive and negative effects for the purpose of NIR phosphorescence. Fig. 15  Normalized emission spectra of [Pt(thpy)(acac)] and [Pt(th2py)(acac)] in CH2Cl2 at 298 K (adapted from ref. 140). 2.3.3 Effect of heteroatoms/heterocycles in the ligand. Complexes containing softer and more polarizable heteroatoms such as sulfur and oxygen incorporated in the ring system of the ligand are much easier to oxidize and have significantly lower transition energies. Therefore, this represents an effective way to achieve a red shift in the corresponding emission spectra. As shown in Fig. 16, the complexes containing the thienyl (X = S) or furyl (X = O) moiety as cyclometalated carbon donor exhibit substantially red-shifted emission compared with the [Pt(phbpy)(CCPh)] (X = CH = CH) complex.129 Fig. 16  Emission spectra of [Pt(X⁁N⁁N)(CCPh)]in alcoholic glass at 77 K (adapted from ref. 129). 2.3.4 Effect of the donor–acceptor (DA) system in the ligand. Introduction of a strong intramolecular DA system in strictly organic small molecules and polymers is a common strategy of lowering the energy gap.3,5,11 If the HOMO levels of the donor and the LUMO levels of the acceptor moiety are close in energy, the resulting band structure will show a low energy gap as depicted in Fig. 17. Recently, a significant amount of work43,143–150 has demonstrated that DA system is a new strategy for phosphorescent transition-metal complexes to achieve the purpose of lowering the energy gap. As shown in Fig. 18, [Pt(DAbpy)(Ph)2] complex with an incorporated DA system shows red-shifted emission when compared with [Pt(D2bpy)(Ph)2] and [Pt(A2bpy)(Ph)2] complexes with only donors or acceptors, respectively.144 It is noteworthy that many of these complexes do not have strong RT phosphorescence, but intense absorption bands in the visible and NIR regions, which might be caused by the fact of non-radiative charge transfer in the DA system. This phenomenon should not limit the applications of such complexes in dye-sensitized solar cells (DSSCs), chemical sensors, and the photogeneration of H2.143–149 Fig. 17  Molecular orbital interaction in donor (D) and acceptor (A) moieties leading to a D–A monomer with an unusually low HOMO–LUMO energy separation (adapted from ref. 3)  Fig. 18  Emission spectra of [Pt(DAbpy)(Ph)2] (red curve), [Pt(A2bpy)(Ph)2] (black curve), and [Pt(D2bpy)(Ph)2] (blue curve) complex in CH2Cl2 at 77K (adapted from ref. 144). 2.3.5 Effect of intramolecular metal–metal interactions. As mentioned previously, multinuclear d8 Pt(II)113,114,151–161 or d10 Au(I)39 complexes can exhibit strong intramolecular metal–metal interactions, leading to red-shifted emission in dilute solution and solid from 3MMLCT. This is another effective strategy for the design of NIR phosphorescent transition-metal complexes. As examples, [(Pt(phbpy))2(μ-dppm)]2+ [dppm = bis(diphenylphosphino)methane, n = 1] with the shortest intramolecular Pt–Pt contact of 3.27 Å shows an obvious red shift in emission spectra compared with [(Pt(phbpy))2(μ-pz)]+ (Pt–Pt contact of 3.61 Å) and [(Pt(phbpy))2(μ-dppp)]2+ [dppp = bis(diphenylphosphino)pentane, n = 5] (Fig. 19).152 Fig. 19  Normalized emission spectra of [(Pt(phbpy))2(μ-dppm)]2+ (red curve), [(Pt(phbpy))2(μ-dppp)]2+ (black curve), and [(Pt(phbpy))2(μ-pz)]+ (blue curve) in MeCN at 77 K (adapted from ref. 152). 2.3.6 Other effects. The effects on the energy gap tuning as discussed above are mainly involved in the individual molecules and oligomers without consideration of any intermolecular interaction. Intermolecular interactions, such as hydrogen bonding, molecular π–π stacking, and charge transfer, also cause a change in the energy gap for the molecules in aggregated states.11,162,163 Square-planar Pt(II) complexes, in particular, can readily form excimers, exciplexes or aggregates,164,165 resulting in red-shifted166,167 or white168–171 emission at high concentration in solution or solid. For example, [Pt(bpy)(Cl)2] (Fig. 6) has two types of stable crystal structures. One is a weakly luminescent yellow form, in which the Pt(II) complexes are spatially well separated from each other (the Pt–Pt distance = 4.44 Å). The other is a strongly red emissive form, in which the square-planar [Pt(bpy)(Cl)2] units stack to form an approximately linear Pt–Pt chain with a metal–metal spacing of 3.45 Å.98–101,172 Another typical example is [Pt(46dFppy)(acac)] complex168–171 [46dFppy = (2-(4,6-difluorophenyl)pyridin)], which can be doped into the host material of 4,4′-N,N-dicarbazole-biphenyl (CBP) to achieve white emission resulting from a combination of blue emission from monomers and yellow emission from excimers, as depicted in Fig. 20. Fig. 20  Emission spectra of [Pt(46dFppy)(acac)]-doped CBP films. 2.4 Methods to achieve high phosphorescence quantum efficiency (ΦP)In principle, emission quantum efficiency (Φ), either ΦF or ΦP, is determined in relation to the radiative rate constant (kr) and the non-radiative rate constant (knr): (1) The rate constants have a strong dependence on the energy gap (ΔE) between the emissive excited state and the ground state, formulated as43,173 (2) (3) The well-known “energy gap law” depicted in eqn (2) indicates that knr increases with a decrease of ΔE (α and β are constant). Amn in eqn (3) is Einstein's A coefficient and Mmn is the emission transition moment between the mth and nth state. Obviously, in order to achieve a red shift in emission, small ΔE is required, which tends to give larger knr and smaller kr, and results in the lower emission quantum yield. This is a leading factor for why efficient NIR emitters have not been well developed. In addition to suppressing the concomitant increase of knr and a decrease of kr with a decreasing ΔE, improving the spin–orbit coupling effect is essential for obtaining efficient NIR phosphorescent materials. Thus, in order to describe ΦP, it is required to consider the nature of the ground state and involved excited states and relevant photophysical interactions among them.71 Empirically, in many cases, the extension of π-conjugation length, the increase of rigidity, or the presence of bulky steric substituents in ligand is beneficial to high ΦP.3. Classification according to the ligandAs discussed above, phosphorescence originating from either 3MLCT or 3ILCT is related to the ligands, thus the aromatic N-donor and/or cyclometalated ligands play an important role in phosphorescence. For easy metalation, the cyclometalated ligand usually has an ancillary donor, such as an N, P atom or a carbene, that can form an initial dative bonding with the transition-metal element, holding the adjacent C–H bond close to the electron rich metal center. Then, intramolecular cleavage of the C–H bond takes place, forming both the metal-containing cyclic framework along with a metal–hydride fragment; the latter then undergoes hydride abstraction leaving only the more stable complex, as depicted in Fig. 21.49 Fig. 21  Schematic pathway of the cyclometalation reaction (D = ancillary donor in the cyclometalated ligand) (adapted from ref. 49). In the literature, there are several reviews about the mechanism of such cyclometalation reactions174–177 and the synthesis of cyclometalated ligands;178 these reviews should be consulted for selecting suitable cyclometalated ligands and transition-metal reagents. According to its coordination number, the aromatic N-donor and/or cyclometalated ligands can be classified into the following categories.3.1 Bidentate ligands3.1.1 N⁁N ligand. The first and classic type of bidentate ligand consists of the bipyridyl framework, such as bpy, 1,10-phenanthroline (phen), 4,7-diphenyl-1,10-phenanthroline (4,7Ph2phen) (Fig. 22), denoted as N⁁N chelate. These ligands are capable of possessing σ-donating nitrogen atoms and π-accepting molecular orbitals to implement the required metal–chelate bonding interaction. These N⁁N ligands can be metalated with many transition-metal atoms, such as Ru(II), Cu(I), Ir(III), and Os(II), to form the corresponding ionic complexes (e.g., [Ru(bpy)3]2+). Such complexes are useful for the application in LECs,40,42,46,55,63 because the emissive layer requires an excess of mobile ions. Alternatively, due to the strong electrostatic attractions of the oppositely charged particles in the condensed phase, these ionic complexes are significantly less volatile and become immiscible in a non-polar matrix, making them poorly suited toward the application in vacuum-deposited OLEDs. Recently, highly emissive neutral Ru(II) and Os(II) complexes for the application in OLEDs were achieved by using the cyclometalated ligand of 2-pyridyl-C-linked azoles, such as pyrazole (pypz), triazole (pytriz) and tetrazole (pytetraz) (Fig. 22).43,49,64 The high acidity of the azole N–H bond makes C-linked 2-pyridyl azoles more reactive and able to extend the scope of the N⁁N cyclometalation reaction. Fig. 22  Chemical structures of some N⁁N, C⁁N, C⁁C, and C⁁P ligands [1-phenyl-3-methylimidazolin-2-ylidene (pmi), 1-phenyl-3-methyl benzimidazolin-2-ylidene (pmb) 1-(2,4-difluorobenzyl)-3-methylbenzimidazolium (24dFb-mb), bz-PPh2, and (2,4-difluorobenzyl)diphenylphosphine (24dFbz-PPh2)]. Metal d6 tris-N⁁N complexes have some disadvantages, however, for instance, [Ru(bpy)3]2+ complexing with asymmetric chelating ligands can have either a facial (fac) or a meridional (mer) configuration. For these cationic Ru(II) diimine complexes, it has been difficult to obtain a specific isomer. Moreover, it is difficult to tune the photophysical properties for this class of ligands, either ppy or pypz, due to their similar electronic and coordinating characteristics.106 Therefore, the combination of the π-accepting pyridyls with oxidizable metals leads to low-energy 3MLCT, as in the infamous red-emitting [Ru(bpy)3]2+ complex.3.1.2 C⁁N cyclometalated ligand. The second and well-known type of bidentate cyclometalated ligand, denoted as C⁁N chelate, consists of 2-phenylpyridyl, such as ppy, 46dFppy, 1-phenylpyrazolyl (ppz), 1-(4,6-difluorophenyl)pyrazolyl (46dFppz), and thpy (Fig. 22).43,49,106,107 These ligands are famous for the metalation of highly emissive neutral Ir(III) complexes for the application in OLEDs. Since this metalation involves removal of a proton from the aromatic heterocycle, the cyclometalated ligands are anionic, in contrary to neutral bpy ligand. Therefore, combining with a π-accepting heterocycle (e.g., pyridine), the strongly σ-donating C⁁N ligands offer very strong M–C covalent interactions as well as highly stabilized ligand-field strength toward the metal ion. Consequently, the energy of MC d–d excited states in these complexes, which generally serves as a major nonradiative deactivating channel, is raised substantially relative to those of neutral diimine ligands such as bpy; the latter tends to yield relatively less luminous metal complexes at RT. As a result, C⁁N cyclometalation offers a good synthetic protocol, yielding efficient luminescent metal complexes. Moreover, the π–π* energy gap of the C⁁N ligand plays an important role in fine-tuning the emission wavelength of the complexes.43,493.1.3 C⁁C and C⁁P cyclometalated ligand. Recently, N-heterocyclic carbenes (NHC)179 and benzyldiphenylphosphines (bz-PPh2),180,181 denoted as C⁁C and C⁁P chelates, respectively, have been introduced as a new variation for cyclometalated chelates (Fig. 22). These π-conjugated or non-conjugated NHC and C⁁P ligands with large enough ligand-centered π–π* energy gaps are good candidates for blue and near ultraviolet-blue phosphorescent transition-metal complexes,180–186 which are out of the focus of this review.3.2 Terdentate ligandDeveloped from bpy, the terdentate ligands, such as tpy (denoted as N⁁N⁁N), phbpy (denoted as C⁁N⁁N), 1,3-di(2-pyridyl)benzene (dpyb) (denoted as N⁁C⁁N), and 2,6-diphenylpyridine (dppy) (denoted as C⁁N⁁C) (Fig. 23) are also well studied.16,43,187–190 Complexes involving d6 metals and the extended π-conjugated terdentate cyclometalated ligands typically have high stability due to the dissociation of the metal ion and the favourable formation of bis-terdentate complexes, resulting in red-shifted absorption and emission spectra. Bis-terdentate d6 complexes offer structural advantages over tris-bipyridyl analogues, being achiral and more amenable to linear extension. Nevertheless, bis-terdentate d6 complexes exhibit distorted octahedral geometry with the poorer bite angles and weaker ligand-field strengths and thus the ΦP is sometimes lower than tris-bidentate d6 complexes. Unlike d6 complexes, d8 complexes, such as Pt(II) complexes, adopt a square-planar geometry, which is favourable for the formation of [Pt(terdentate)(L)]n+ complexes with high ΦP.187–190 Moreover, as discussed in Section 2.3.5, this kind of multinuclear Pt(II) complexes might exhibit strong intramolecular metal–metal interactions, which represent another effective strategy for the design of NIR phosphorescent transition-metal complexes. Fig. 23  Chemical structures of bpy and some terdentate ligands. 3.3 Tetradentate ligandTetradentate ligands (Fig. 24), acting as chelating reagents, can react with many metal ions and result in stable metal complexes. For example, robust porphyrins and metalloporphyrins are highly luminescent with varying electronic structures and interesting excited-state properties for applications in phosphorescent LEDs,77,191–193 field-effect transistors (FETs),194–198 sensors,38,199–201 and PVCs.202 With more planar geometry than porphyrins, phthalocyanines (Pc) and metallophthalocyanines have big π–π stacks and strong absorption with less emission, making them suitable for the applications in FETs203,204 and PVCs.41,202,205 With the exception of Pc complexes, the Pt(II) complexes with the terdentate ligands of 2,2′:6′,2′′:6′′,2′′′-quaterpyridine (qtpy),206N,N-bis(salicylidene)-1,2-ethylenediamine (Salen),126,133,207–209 and porphyrin77,191–193 usually are highly phosphorescent, like Pt(II) complexes with the terdentate cyclometalated ligands. Furthermore, extending the π-conjugated system of porphyrin ligands in Pt(II) complexes represents an efficient way to achieve NIR phosphorescence, as discussed in Section 4.7.3. Fig. 24  Chemical structures of some tetradentate ligands. 4. Classification according to the central metal atom4.1 Cr(III) complexesThere are several comprehensive reviews detailing the photophysics and photochemistry of Cr(III) complexes.210–213 Their phosphorescence (2E → 4A2) is predominantly restricted to the red and NIR spectral region, due to the small excited-ground state energy gaps in the first row transition-metal complexes. Like [Ru(bpy)3]2+ complex, [Cr(bpy)3]3+ (Cr1) and its analogous complexes are the most investigated system among the Cr(III) complexes.214–220 For example, [Cr(phen)3]3+ (Cr2) emits NIR phosphorescence (λem = 730 nm) and can oxidize the guanine base of DNA, resulting in phosphorescence quenching, as shown in Fig. 25. Since guanine oxidation has been shown to serve as a genesis point for DNA strand scission, these Cr(III) complexes show potential as a new class of DNA photocleavage agents (photonucleases) (Fig. 26).213,221 Fig. 25  Steady-state emission spectrum of an air-saturated 3.3 × 10−5 M solution of Cr2 in 50 mM Tris-HCl buffer (pH 7.1) in the presence of different concentrations of calf thymus B-DNA (wavelength of excitation = 320 nm) (adapted from ref. 219).  Fig. 26  Photoactivated cleavage at pH 7.4 of 5 μM ΦX174 plasmid DNA by 200 μM Cr2 following irradiation at 350 nm (Rayonet). Samples were subjected to electrophoresis on 1% agarose gels for 3h at 70 V, followed by staining with ethidium bromide (adapted from ref. 213). Table 1 summarizes the photophysical properties of some NIR phosphorescent Cr(III) complexes (Cr1–Cr32) [Cr3 = [Cr(4,4Ph2bpy)3]3+ (4,4Ph2bpy = 4,4′-diphenyl-2,2′-bipyridine), Cr4 = [Cr(4,4Me2bpy)3]3+ (4,4Me2bpy = 4,4′-dimethyl-2,2′-bipyridine), Cr5 = [Cr(5Clphen)3]3+ (5Clphen = 5-chloro-1,10-phenanthroline), Cr6 = [Cr(4,7Me2phen)3]3+ (4,7Me2phen = 4,7-dimethyl-1,10-phenanthroline), Cr7 = [Cr(4,7Ph2phen)3]3+, Cr8 = [Cr(3,4,7,8Me4phen)3]3+ (3,4,7,8Me4phen = 3,4,7,8-tetramethyl-1,10-phenanthroline), Cr9 = [Cr(tpy)2]3+, Cr10 = [Cr(phen)(bpy)2]3+, Cr11 = [Cr(phen)2(bpy)]3+, Cr12 = [Cr(phen)(3,4,7,8Me4phen)2]3+, Cr13 = [Cr(phen)2(3,4,7,8Me4phen)]3+, Cr14 = [Cr(phen)2(DPPZ)]3+ (DPPZ = dipyridophenazine), Cr15 = [trans-Cr(en)2(SCN)2]+, Cr16 = [trans-Cr(en)2(NH3)(SCN)]2+, Cr17 = [trans-Cr(en)2(F)2]+, Cr18 = [trans-Cr(en)2(SCN)(F)]+, Cr19 = [Cr(en)3]3+, Cr20 = [trans-Cr(NH3)4(SCN)2]+, Cr21 = [trans-Cr(en)2(NH3)(F)]2+, Cr22 = [Cr(NH3)5(SCN)]2+, Cr23 = [cis-Cr(NH3)4(SCN)2]+, Cr24 = [trans-Cr(NH3)4(SCN)(F)]+, Cr25 = [cis-Cr(en)2(NH3)(F)]2+, Cr26 = [trans-Cr(NH3)2(SCN)4]−, Cr27 = [cis-Cr(en)2(CN)2]+, Cr28 = [trans-Cr(en)2(NH3)(Cl)]2+, Cr29 = [Cr(NH3)5(F)]2+, Cr30 = [Cr(SCN)6]3−, Cr31 = [cis-Cr(en)2(NH3)(Cl)]2+, Cr32 = [Cr(CN)6]3−] at RT. Due to non-radiative decay processes, including intersystem crossing to the ground state, or back intersystem crossing to the 4T2 state,213,218 the phosphorescent quantum efficiencies for most of these complexes are too low (<10−3) for practical applications. However, it is noteworthy that while photodamage in the analogous Ru(II) cases is dramatically decreased in the absence of O2 (consistent with a singlet O2 pathway), photodamage by Cr2 is considerably greater under a N2 atmosphere. Since many cancer cells are hypoxic, the increase in photodamage at lower O2 levels may provide a selectivity advantage for these [Cr(diimine)3]3+ reagents in terms of their future potential as phototherapeutic agents.213 Therefore, there is ample room for the further development of these [Cr(N⁁N)3]3+ complexes.

Copper complexes, in which the copper ion has two common oxidation states: Cu+ (d10) and Cu2+ (d9), have attracted significant attention considering their high relative abundance, low cost, and nontoxic properties compared to noble metal complexes. Clearly, the complete filling of d orbitals prevents MC d–d electronic transitions in Cu(I) complexes. Such transitions are available to d9 Cu(II) complexes, however, and they are deactivated via ultrafast non-radiative processes. Accordingly, the most luminescent Cu complexes are not Cu(II) complexes but Cu(I) complexes.37,76 The coordination behavior of Cu(I) complexes is strictly related to its electronic configuration. The complete filling of d orbitals (d10 configuration) leads to a symmetric localization of the electronic charge, which favors a tetrahedral disposition of the ligands around the metal center in order to locate the coordinative sites far from one another and minimize electrostatic repulsions (Fig. 27). However, these four-coordinated Cu(I) complexes readily suffer a Jahn–Teller based distortion (flattening) in the excited state, and consequent formation of a five-coordinated exciplex, which promotes nonradiative decay (Fig. 27).222 Therefore, luminescent Cu(I) complexes, [Cu(N⁁N)2]+223–228 or [Cu(N⁁N)(P⁁P)]+228–232 complexes, usually have sterically bulky substituents on the ligands to avoid this distortion. As a result, some [Cu(N⁁N)2]+ (Cu1–Cu11)223–225,227 and [Cu(N⁁N)(P⁁P)]+ (Cu12)228 complexes emit weak NIR phosphorescence (3MLCT, ΦP < 10−2) at RT (Fig. 28 and Table 2). Recently, changing the geometry of Cu(I) complexes from four-coordinated into three-coordinated configuration was demonstrated to be an alternative strategy to solve the problem of distortion.233–235 Fig. 27  Flattening distortion and subsequent nucleophilic attack by solvent, counterion, or other molecules following light excitation in Cu(I) complexes.  Fig. 28  Chemical structures of some NIR phosphorescent Cu(I) complexes (Cu1–Cu14).

complexes are not highly emissive and thus it is not unexpected to find that the Cu(II) Pc complex ([Cu(Pc)]) (Cu15) (Fig. 29) exhibits only weak NIR phosphorescence in 1-chloronaphthalene (NaphCl) (Cu15 is not soluble in most solvents and only partially soluble in NaphCl or 1,2-dichlorobenzene236) at 77 K (ΦP = 1 × 10−3) and 300 K (ΦP = 1 × 10−4).237,238 However, due to their strong absorption, high charge mobility, good thermal and morphological stability, Cu15 and its derivatives have received great attention in a variety of applications, such as NIR emitter239–243 and hole-injecting layer244,245 in OLEDs, organic semiconductor in TFTs,246,247 donor or dye in PVCs.202,248,249 For example, Cu15 can be co-doped with an Ir(III) complex, which serves as a red phosphorescent sensitizer, in the host material of CBP to enhance the NIR electroluminescence (EL) [λEL = 1120 nm with an external quantum efficiency (EQE) of 0.1%] by 15 times over the Cu15 monodoped device.243 Fig. 29  Chemical structure and EL spectrum of Cu15 (adapted from ref. 243). 4.2.2 Multinuclear Cu(I) complexes. A remarkable feature of the molecular structures of multinuclear d10 metal complexes, such as Cu(I) complexes, is the observation of short metal–metal distances. Theoretically, in the absence of (n + 1)s and (n + 1)p functions, interactions between the closed-shell d10 centres are repulsive in nature. However, configuration mixing of the filled nd-orbitals with the empty orbitals derived from higher energy (n + 1)s and (n + 1)p orbitals has been proposed to establish some weak metal–metal interactions.39 The clusters of Cu(I) complexes, especially cubane-like [Cu4(X)4(L)4] clusters,250 have attracted considerable interest because of their dual emission and long phosphorescent τ.251–254There are several examples of NIR phosphorescent [Cu4(X)4(L)4] clusters (Cu16–Cu22)255,256 (Fig. 30 and Table 2) reported by Ford et al. X-Ray crystallography shows that these complexes have a “cubane” Cu4I4 core with a single pyridine (py) bound to each Cu and short Cu–Cu distances (ave. 2.69 Å) despite the absence of formal covalent bonding between these d10 centers in the ground state.257 Interestingly, some of these clusters show a phenomenon of “luminescence thermochromism”. As an example, for Cu16 at high temperature, the strong low-energy emission (λem = 690 nm) is dominant and assigned to a triplet cluster-centered (3CC) transition with the combination of iodide to copper charge transfer (3XMCT) and d–s transitions. At low temperature, however, this emission is replaced by the weak high-energy band (λem = 480 nm), assigned to a triplet halide to ligand charge transfer (3XLCT) (Fig. 30).250 Thus, lowering the temperature leads to dramatic changes in the luminescence color, which even can be detected with the naked eyes and is useful for rapid visual thermal sensing. When the aromatic amine ligand pyridine is replaced by the aliphatic piperidine (Cu21) or morpholine (Cu22), the corresponding cluster preserves the low-energy 3CC emission but lacks the high-energy 3XLCT emission owing to the absence of ligands possessing π orbitals. Fig. 30  Chemical structures of some NIR phosphorescent tetrahedral Cu(I) complexes (Cu16–Cu21) and temperature dependence of the emission spectra of Cu16 in toluene (adapted from ref. 255). In addition to cubane-like tetrahedral Cu(I) complexes, Yam et al. described a series of novel saddle-like tetranuclear Cu(I)-phosphinidene complexes, [Cu4(dppm)4(S)]2+,258 [Cu4(dppm)4(Se)]2+,259 and [Cu4(dppm)4(PPh)]2+260 (Cu23) (Fig. 31), which emit intense long-lived yellow-orange, orange-red, and NIR luminescence, respectively, originating predominantly from the LMCT with mixing of a MC (d–s/d–p) state. Such an assignment is consistent with the trend observed in the luminescence energy with S2− > Se2− > PPh2− and Ag(I) > Cu(I).57 It should be noted that the Cu–Cu distances in these tetrahedral Cu(I)–phosphinidene complexes range from 2.82 to 3.44 Å, resulting in no significant Cu–Cu interactions. Another type of NIR phosphorescent tetranuclear Cu(I) alkynyl complexes (Cu24–Cu31)261 (Fig. 31) with an “open-cube” structure and Cu–Cu distances of 2.46–2.48 Å were reported by the same group. The alkynyl group has many advantages, such as the linear geometry, structural rigidity, extended π-electron delocalization, π-unsaturated nature, and ability to interact with metal centers via pπ–dπ overlap, for building blocks in the construction of carbon-rich metal-containing optical materials. At RT, like [Cu4(X)4(L)4] clusters, most of these complexes, in solid state, displayed dual emission behavior with high-energy emission (443 < λem < 541 nm) and low-energy emission (623 < λem < 665 nm), originating from the IL π–π* (alkynyl ligand)/MLCT [Cu → π*(RCC)] and the LMCT [RCC → Cu4]/Cu(I)-centered d–s/d–p characters, respectively. In contrast, all of the open-cube complexes in solution exhibit only a single structure-less emission band centered at 665–700 nm (Table 2), which is red shifted compared to the solid-state samples of the respective complexes due to the presence of rigidochromism effects. Fig. 31  Perspective view of Cu23 with atomic numbering scheme (the phenyl rings of the dppm ligand are omitted for clarity) and chemical structures of some NIR phosphorescent tetranuclear Cu(I) alkynyl complexes (Cu24–Cu31). In addition to tetranuclear Cu(I) complexes, NIR phosphorescent dinuclear (Cu32–Cu34),262–264 hexanuclear (Cu35–Cu37),265,266 and polymeric (Cu38 and Cu39)264,267 Cu(I) complexes (Fig. 32) are reported. An interesting report264 by Tsuge et al. demonstrated with a series of insoluble Cu(I) complexes, formulated as [Cu2(X)2(PPh3)(L)n] (X = Br or I and L = N-heteroaromatic ligands), that their strong emissions can be finely tuned from blue to red and NIR (Cu34 and Cu38) region (Fig. 33). The emission is attributed to 3MLCT, because there are no strong Cu–Cu interactions (2.87–3.30 Å). Moreover, since the effects of bridging halides are smaller than those of N-heteroaromatic ligands, the energy of the emission can be varied by increasing the electron-accepting character of the ligand L. Recently, Hong et al. prepared two hexanuclear Cu(I) clusters [(Cu6(4-ptt)6]·3DMF·3H2O (Cu36) and [(Cu6(4-ptt)6]·8DMF·7H2O (Cu37)266 [4-Hptt = 5-(pyridin-4-yl)-1H-1,2,4-triazole-3-thiol, DMF = N,N′-dimethylformamide] by the differing reaction temperatures. The Cu6S6 units in Cu36 pack in a polydirectional array, while they extend in one direction in Cu37. Their varied Cu–Cu interactions (Cu36: 2.85–3.08 Å, Cu37: 3.13–3.32 Å) and individual packing modes cause differences in luminescent and thermostable behaviors. Cu36 exhibits an unusually long wavelength at 904 nm and a higher thermal stability; while the emission (λem = 776 nm) of Cu37, like [Cu4(X)4(L)4] clusters, splits into two bands (λem = 589 and 871 nm) as the temperature decreases. Therefore, the emission of Cu36 originates from a 3CC transition, and that of Cu37 comes from a mixture of 3CC and 3MLCT. Fig. 32  Chemical structures of some NIR phosphorescent Cu(I) complexes (Cu32–Cu39).  Fig. 33  Emission spectra of [Cu2(X)2(PPh3)(L)n] complexes in solid at RT: dmap = N,N-dimethyl-4-aminopyridine, 4,4bpy = 4,4′-bipyridine, 1,6-nap = 1,6-naphthyridine, and pyz = pyrazine (adapted from ref. 264). Table 2 summarizes the photophysical properties of some NIR phosphorescent Cu(I) complexes at RT. As one of first row transition metals, copper exerts a negligible heavy atom effect and the resulting complexes usually have too low phosphorescent quantum efficiency or too low solubility for practical applications. Nonetheless, the need for abundant, cheap, and environmentally friendly smart materials makes copper compounds an attractive alternative to more traditional choices based on precious metals. The current trends in literature indeed suggest that Cu(I) complexes are attracting increasing attention for technological applications (e.g., OLEDs),76,235,253 although we are still at the level of prototypes and proof-of-principle, further important breakthroughs may be anticipated in the years to come.4.3 Re(I) and Re(III) complexes4.3.1 Re(I) complexes. Among d6 Re(I) complexes, tricarbonylRe(I) diimine complexes268 of the type [Re(N⁁N)(CO)3(L)]n+ (N⁁N = diimine ligand, L = monodentate ligand and n = 0 or 1) are the best studied and are known to exhibit RT phosphorescence, which is highly sensitive to the nature of the environment and can be used as photoswitchable materials, chemical probes and photocleavage agents.269–271 Most of their emission spectra are often found to occur near 600 nm and are structure-less and assignable to a 3MLCT state, because carbonyl ligands undergo significant π back-bonding with the metal-centre. Consequently, the energy gap between the metal d orbitals and the π* ligand orbitals involved in the 3MLCT transition is small enough for acting as low-energy emission. Their quantum yields are typically small (∼10−3) compared with complexes of the later transition metals. Nonetheless, some [Re(N⁁N)(CO)3(CNR)]+ (N⁁N = bpy or phen, R = t-Bu or n-alkyl) exhibit exceptionally high ΦP (> 0.7) and long τ (>100 μs) in fluid solutions at RT.272 This series of complexes is particularly interesting as their emission wavelengths are centred in the 450–500 nm region with strong vibronic character, consistent with the diimine ligand emission. Since the emission envelope of the coordinated diimine ligand is red-shifted from that of the free ligand, a combination of 3ILCT (3π–π* diimine) and 3MLCT state is often employed to account for the emission behavior. After that, much attention has been focused on improving their RT phosphorescence and thermal and photochemical stability for the applications in OLEDs, TTAU, and charge transfer.273–278Meyer et al. succeeded in tuning the emission band of the “parent complexes” [Re(bpy)(CO)3(Cl)] [λem = 642 nm in 2-methyltetrahydrofuran (MeTHF)] by the introduction of electron-donating [e.g., –NH2 (λem = 575 nm), –NEt2 (λem = 573 nm), –NHCOMe (λem = 620 nm), –OMe (λem = 630 nm), or –Me (λem = 626 nm)] and electron-withdrawing [e.g., –Ph (λem = 647 nm), –Cl (Re1) (λem = 700 nm), –CO2Et (Re2) (λem = 715 nm), or –NO2 (Re3) (λem = 780 nm)] substituents onto the 4,4′-positions of bpy ligand (Fig. 34 and Table 3).279 Yam and co-workers demonstrated two [Re(bpy)(CO)3(Cl)] derivatives (Re4 and Re5),280 equipped with spirooxazine-containing bpy ligands, that emitted strong RT phosphorescence originating form 3dπ(Re) → π*(diimine). Moreover, the emission properties of the complexes could readily be switched by opening the oxazine through photochromic reactions. W. K. Chan et al. also reported a series of NIR phosphorescent [Re(N⁁N)(CO)3(Cl)] (Re6–Re16)273,281,282 complexes and polymers for photovoltaic and light-emitting applications, but the EQEs of the resulting OLEDs were found to be low (10−3–10−4). Fig. 34  Chemical structures of some NIR phosphorescent [Re(N⁁N)(CO)3(Cl)] complexes (Re1–Re16).

μsLCCT301Re84Solid—680——LCCT302Re84@SiO2Solid—700——LCCT302Re85Solid—675——LCCT302Re85@SiO2Solid—690——LCCT302Re86Solid—750——LCCT302Re86@SiO2Solid—760——LCCT302Re87Solid—800——LCCT302Re87@SiO2Solid—700——LCCT302Yam and co-workers used acetylide to replace the –Cl in the [Re(N⁁N)(CO)3(Cl)] complexes and obtained a series of NIR phosphorescent mononuclear283 or binuclear284 Re(I) diimine acetylide complexes (Fig. 35). The introduction of acetylide moieties into the d6 metal diimine system has the added advantage of rendering the metal center more electron rich, thus raising the energy of the d–d states, which improves the population of the MLCT state. The addition of one –CC– unit leads to a red shift of the transition energies of ca. 0.24 eV. Interestingly, when Re22 with the –CC–CC– unit is excited at 350–400 nm, a long-lived luminescence at ca. 660–690 nm results, probably 3MLCT [dπ(Re) → π*(t-Bu2bpy)] in origin. If the same complex is excited at ≥450 nm, an exceptionally lower lying emission at ca. 750 nm appears and is contributed to 3MLCT [dπ(Re) → π*(CC–CC–Re)] or 3LLCT [π(CC–CC–Re) → π*(t-Bu2bpy)]. Moreover, the introduction of a triarylboron moiety to alkynylRe(I) tricarbonyl diimine complexes (Re23–Re25)285 allows for the binding of F− ions with resulting spectral changes in the UV-vis absorption and emission spectra (Fig. 36); such complexes may find interesting applications in the design of spectrochemical and luminescence chemosensors. The further work of the same group reveals that the –Cl in the [Re(N⁁N)(CO)3(Cl)] complexes can be replaced by pyridinyl derivatives (Re26 and Re27),286 as depicted in Fig. 35. Addition of alkali-metal or alkaline-earth-metal ions to a MeOH solution of Re27 results in a blue shift of the absorption band from 410 to 380 nm, indicating the binding of the cations to the polyether cavity. In addition to bipyridin ligand, other N⁁N ligands, such as dipyrrin ligands, are used to synthesize NIR phosphorescent (λem = 688–711 nm) Re(I) complexes (Re28–Re34),287 in which one of three CO groups can be further replaced by PPh3 or PBu3 group, but resulting in much lower emissive complexes. Fig. 35  Chemical structures of some NIR phosphorescent [Re(N⁁N)(CO)3(X)] complexes (Re17–Re34).  Fig. 36  Photograph showing the color change of Re24 before (left) and after (right) the addition of fluoride ion (adapted from ref. 285). 4.3.2 Re(III) complexes. Hexanuclear Re(III) chalcohalide clusters can serve as promising “building blocks” for the creation of new porous frameworks, nanoparticles and mesoporous materials. Because of the controllable architectures and multianchoring sites of clusters together with their photoluminescence, Re(III) cluster complexes are promising potential candidates as catalysts, chemical color sensors, magnetic materials, gas storage materials, optoelectronic, diagnostic agents, and gene/drug carriers.288–290 In 1999, three research groups including Sasaki,291,292 Nocera,293 and Batail294 reported independently that 24-electron [Re6(Q)8(X)6]z clusters (Re35–Re51) (Q = S2−, Se2−, or Te2−, L = Cl−, Br−, I− CN−, PEt3, MeCN, DMF, or pyridine, [Re6(S)8(Cl)6]4− = Re35, [Re6(S)8(Br)6]4− = Re36, [Re6(S)8(I)6]4− = Re37, [Re6(S)8(PEt3)6]2+ = Re38, [Re6(Se)8(MeCN)6]2+ = Re39, [Re6(Se)8(py)6]2+ = Re40, [Re6(Se)8(DMF)6]2+ = Re41, trans-[Re6(Se)8(PEt3)4(I)2] = Re42, cis-[Re6(Se)8(PEt3)4(I)2] = Re43, [Re6(Se)8(PEt3)5(I)]+ = Re44, [Re6(Se)8(PEt3)5(Br)]+ = Re45, trans-[Re6(S)8(PEt3)4(Br)2] = Re46, cis-[Re6(S)8(PEt3)4(Br)2] = Re47, mer-[Re6(S)8(PEt3)3(Br)3]− = Re48, [Re6(S)8(CN)6]4− = Re49, [Re6(Se)8(CN)6]4− = Re50, and [Re6(Te)8(CN)6]4− = Re51) as well as the paramagnetic 23-electron cluster trianion [Re6S8Cl6]3− (Re52) (Fig. 37) show RT NIR phosphorescence both in solution and crystalline phases with τ and ΦP up to 18.9 μs and 0.203, respectively. As shown in Fig. 38, the absorption and emission spectra of [Re6(S)8(X)6]4− [X = Cl− (Re35), Br− (Re36), or I− (Re37)] exhibit red shift along the series Cl < Br < I, which agrees with ligand-to-cluster ([Re6(Q)8]2+) charge transfer (LCCT).295 Since then many other σ-donor ligands have been used to develop new NIR phosphorescent hexanuclear Re(III) clusters, such as (trans-[Re6(Se)8(PEt3)4(CN)2] (Re53), [Re6(Se)8(PEt3)5(CN)]+ (Re54), trans-[Re6(Se)8(PEt3)4(MeCN)2]2+ (Re55), [Re6(Se)8(PEt3)5(MeCN)]+ (Re56), [Re6(Se)8(PEt3)4(DMSO)2]2+ (Re57), [Re12Se16(PEt3)10(μ-4,4bpy)]4+ (Re58),295trans-[Re6(S)8(Cl)4(pyz)2]2− (Re59), cis-[Re6(S)8(Cl)4(pyz)2]2− (Re60), trans-[Re6(S)8(Cl)4(4,4bpy)2]2− (Re61), cis-[Re6(S)8(Cl)4(4,4bpy)2]2− (Re62), trans-[Re6(S)8(Cl)4(py)2]2− (Re63), cis-[Re6(S)8(Cl)4(py)2]2− (Re64), mer-[Re6(S)8(Cl)3(py)3]2− (Re65), trans-[Re6(S)8(Cl)4(4Mepy)2]2− (Re66) (4Mepy = 4-methylpyridine), cis-[Re6(S)8(Cl)4(4(Me2N)py)2]2− (Re67) (4(Me2N)py = 4-(dimethylamino)pyridine),296 [Re6(Se)8(I)4(μ-dpph)]2− (Re68) (dpph = PPh2(CH2)6PPh2), trans-[Re6(Se)8(I)2(μ-dpph)2] (Re69), cis-[Re6(Se)8(I)2(μ-dpph)2] (Re70), [Re6(Se)8(μ-dpph)3]2+ (Re71),297 [Re6(S)8(NCS)6]4− (Re72), and [Re6(Se)8(NCS)6]4− (Re73).298 Further studies showed that the Q of the [Re6(Q)8]2+ core also can be replaced by Br− to obtain chalcobromide-capped clusters including fac-[Re6(S7Br)(PPh3)3(Br)3] (Re74), fac-[Re6(Se7Br)(PPh3)3(Br)3] (Re75),299 [Re6(Se7Br)(Br)6]3− (Re76), D3d-[Re6(Se6Br2)(Br)6]2− (Re77), C2v-[Re6(Se6Br2)(Br)6]2− (Re78), [Re6(Se5Br3)(Br)6]− (Re79), [Re6(S7Br)(Br)6]3− (Re80), D3d-[Re6(S6Br2)(Br)6]2− (Re81) and C2v-[Re6(S6Br2)(Br)6]2− (Re82),300 which emit NIR phosphorescence with λem up to 968 nm (Table 3). Fig. 37  Structure of hexanuclear Re(III) chalcohalide clusters, [Re6Q8L6]z.  Fig. 38  Absorption and emission spectra of [Re6(S)8(X)6]4− clusters [X=Cl− (Re35), Br− (Re36), or I− (Re37)] in dichloromethane at RT (adapted from ref. 295). Table 3 summarizes the photophysical properties of some NIR phosphorescent Re(I) and Re(III) complexes at RT. Re(I) complexes usually possess low quantum yields (∼10−3), which limits their applications as NIR luminophores. Re(III) cluster complexes constitute a kind of very promising luminophore candidate for biotechnology applications because they are nanosized entities offering robust NIR phosphorescence. However, they cannot be used directly for biological applications because of potential toxic effects and quenching of the clusters' luminescence in aqueous media. Therefore, some Re(III) cluster complexes, such as [Re6(Se)8(4-t-butylpyridine)4(methacrylic acid)2] (Re83), [Re6(S)8(OH)6]4− (Re84), [Re6(Se)8(OH)6]4− (Re85), [Re6(S)8(CN)4(OH)2]4− (Re86), and [Re6(S)8(Br)6]4− (Re87), need to be dispersed in a biocompatible matrix such as poly(methyl methacrylate) (PMMA)301 or SiO2 nanoparticles,302 resulting in highly NIR luminescent transparent pellets or aqueous solutions (Fig. 39); such formulations have a bright future for multiple applications. Fig. 39  (a) Digital photographs of the hybrid PMMA containing covalently linked Re76 clusters. Top: under normal daylight; bottom: under UV irradiation. (b) Aqueous solution of Re77 clusters (I) before and (II) after embedding in silica nanoparticles, under daylight (left picture) and under UV irradiation at 365 nm (right picture) (adapted from ref. 301 and 302). 4.4 Ru(II) complexes4.4.1 Ru(II) complexes with bidentate ligands. Studies into the photochemistry and photophysics of Ru(II) complexes have undergone an impressive growth in the last few decades. The prototype complex [Ru(bpy)3]2+ (λem = 606 nm, ΦP = 0.062, τ = 870 ns in degassed MeCN) or [Ru(phen)3]2+ (λem = 587 nm, ΦP = 0.023, τ = 340 ns in degassed EtOH) has certainly been one of the molecules most extensively studied and widely used in research laboratories during the last 30 years.60–64 As discussed in Section 3.1, phosphorescence (ΦP < 0.1, τ < 10 μs) is frequently detected in RT fluid solution from these complexes and is attributed to low-lying 3MLCT excited states. Since the ligand field splitting of the Ru(II) complexes has an intermediate value between the Fe(II) (non-emissive) and Os(II)/Ir(III) (highly emissive) cases, the MC levels of Ru(II) complexes that are thermally accessible contribute to deactivation of the MLCT emissive level, resulting in ΦP < 0.1. Ligand tuning is largely ineffective and emission wavelengths are generally restricted to the orange–red spectral region. In order to red shift the emission of [Ru(bpy)3]2+ or [Ru(phen)3]2+ into the NIR region, electron-withdrawing groups, –CO2R (R = –H,303 –Me, –C2H5, or –CH2Ph304) or –CN,305 or electron-donating groups, –NEt2,306 –CC–Ph,307–309 or polythiophene,310 must be introduced on the bpy or phen ligand (Ru1–Ru13) (Fig. 40 and Table 4). The noteworthy example from Zhao's group, Ru11, has a very long τ of 58.4 μs, which does not originate from 3MLCT, but 3IL/3LLCT (vide infra) and has an excellent performance in oxygen sensing (improved by 150 fold over [Ru(bpy)2(phen)]2+) and TTAU (upconversion quantum yield = 0.096) (Fig. 41).308,309 In addition to bpy and phen ligands, some other N⁁N,311–314 C⁁N,315 or P313 ligands (Fig. 42) are also used to prepare mononuclear or multinuclear Ru(II) complexes (Ru14–Ru34). Nonetheless, these complexes have too low ΦP values for applications (Table 4). For example, Ru27314 can be used in LECs to emit NIR phosphorescence at λem = 945 nm with a low efficiency of 3 × 10−4. Fig. 40  Chemical structures of some NIR phosphorescent Ru(II) complexes (Ru1–Ru13).
