Fig. 42  Chemical structures of some NIR phosphorescent Ru(II) complexes (Ru14–Ru40). Although some Ru(II) complexes with carboxylated bipyridine (Ru1 and Ru35–Ru40316) or multipyridine ligands (see Section 4.4.2) usually have low NIR ΦP at RT (Table 4), they can be chemically anchored onto TiO2 nanoparticles by carboxyl groups and have shown the best photovoltaic properties: a broad absorption spectrum in visible and NIR region, suitable excited and ground state energy levels, relatively long excited-state lifetimes, and good electrochemical stability. Several Ru(II) bipyridine complexes including Ru4053,61 (called N3 or “red dye”) used in DSSCs have reached more than 10% overall conversion efficiency under standard measurement conditions.4.4.2 Ru(II) complexes with terdentate or tetradentate ligands. As discussed in Section 3.2, the tridentate tpy-type ligands offer several synthetic and structural advantages compared to the bidentate bpy-type ligands, but [Ru(tpy)2]2+ (non-emissive, τ = 250 ps measured by transient absorption spectroscopy) exhibits less favourable photophysical properties (in particular, lack of luminescence and very short excited state lifetime at RT) than [Ru(bpy)3]2+.317 Many efforts have been devoted to the design and synthesis of terdentate polypyridine ligands to improve the photophysical properties of resultant Ru(II) complexes. The typical approach is to take advantage of the substituent effects for tuning the energy gap of 3MLCT and 3MC states, which involves the insertion of judiciously selected heterocyclic substituents onto the tpy chelate. Increasing the ligand bite angle also can be attempted to make the complex adopt a less distorted octahedral arrangement and hence increase the ligand field strength and reduce the nonradiative decay. As an example of substituent effects, the presence of electron-withdrawing or -donating substituents on tpy leads to a red shift in emission into the NIR region (Ru41–Ru48)318 (Fig. 43 and Table 4); this results from a destabilization of the metal-based HOMO to a greater extent than the ligand-based LUMO. Moreover, electron-withdrawing substituents increase the luminescence quantum yield and the excited state lifetime, whereas electron-donating substituents have an opposite effect because the electron-donating substituents do not destabilise the 3MC state to a great extent and nonradiative decay back to the ground state is facilitated. Acetylene substituents also can be used to tune photophysical properties of [Ru(tpy)2]2+-type complexes (Ru49–Ru53).317 The acetylene groups grafted directly onto the terpyridine core can enhance electron delocalisation and stabilise the ligand-based LUMO, resulting in an increase in the energy gap between 3MLCT and 3MC and a subsequent reduction in the mixing of the 3MLCT state with the 3MC state. Moreover, an increase in the number of acetylene units was found to decrease the emission energy and increase the excited state lifetime (Ru50: λem = 690 nm, τ = 55 ns; Ru51: λem = 710 nm, τ = 170 ns). An alternative approach to prolong the lifetime of [Ru(tpy)2]2+-type complexes was achieved by the introduction of electron-withdrawing pyrimidine groups (Ru54–Ru66)319–321 onto tpy ligands. Unlike phenyl groups, pyrimidine groups can act as coplanar substituents through intramolecular hydrogen bonding to enhance electron delocalization in the ligand and enlarge the energy gap between 3MLCT and 3MC, resulting in relatively long τ for Ru63320 (τ = 1.81 μs). Fig. 43  Chemical structures of some NIR phosphorescent Ru(II) complexes (Ru41–Ru82). Since most [Ru(tpy)2]2+-type complexes (Ru41–Ru61) usually have low ΦP or short τ at RT, many other N⁁N⁁N ligands, such as 2,6-bis(8′-quinolinyl)pyridine (bqp) (Ru67–Ru70),322,323 2-phenyl-4,6-dipyridin-2-yl-1,3,5-triazine (trz) (Ru71 and Ru72),324 2,4-di(pyridin-2-yl)pyrimidine (dpyp) (Ru73 and Ru74),325 and bpy-X-pyrazole (X = CH2 or CO) (Ru75–Ru77),326 or NCS ligands (Ru78–Ru82)327 are used to prepare NIR phosphorescent Ru(II) complexes (Fig. 43). Remarkably, Ru67322 with bqp ligand exhibits near octahedral structure and demonstrates the best photophysical properties (λem = 700 nm, ΦP = 0.02, τ = 3.0 μs) reported up to now among the bis-tridentate Ru(II) polypyridine complex. The capability of the bqp ligand to form double hexagonal metallacycles is a likely explanation as this could relieve the structural distortion from the ideal octahedral geometry upon excitation. The further work in the same group of Hammarstrom reveals that the presence of electron-withdrawing –CO2Et substituents in Ru68323 can improve ΦP and τ up to 0.07 and 5.5 μs, respectively. The NIR EL of Ru71 and Ru72324 is observed at λEL = 717 and 725 nm, respectively, but the EQE, around 0.005% in both LECs, is very low. Ru80327 (called N719 or “black dye”) with tpy ligand has a broader absorption in the NIR region than Ru40 with bpy ligand, resulting in an overall conversion efficiency of 10.4% for Ru80-based DSSCs.Multinuclear or heteronuclear Ru(II) complexes containing N⁁N⁁N ligands that can absorb and emit light in the NIR region are the subject of intensive research in many fields, especially in photoactive molecular-scale wires or supermolecules.60,63,328 For example, [Ru(tpy)2]2+ units can be spaced by acetylide and/or [Zn(tpy)2]2+ (Ru83–Ru88)328 (Fig. 44) to emit NIR emission with τ up to 720 ns. A recent report reveals that the emission from “molecular wires” of Ru89–Ru92329 can be tuned at λem = 758, 1040, 1052, and 1078 nm, respectively, which agrees with the increase of nuclearity from two to six. However, these multinuclear or heteronuclear Ru(II) complexes generally have very low ΦP values and will not be discussed further in this review. Interested readers should consult some available reviews60,63,328 to obtain in-depth mechanistic discussions and information on multinuclear or heteronuclear Ru(II) complexes. Fig. 44  Chemical structures of some NIR phosphorescent Ru(II) complexes (Ru83–Ru92). Emissive Ru(II) complexes containing tetradentate ligands are rare because presumably Ru(II) complexes with tetradentate ligands, such as qtpy, have a highly distorted octahedral structure. For example, [Ru(N⁁N⁁N⁁N)(X)2] (X = –Cl or –NCS) complexes (Ru93–Ru99)330 (Fig. 45) show weak and broad emission signals with onset at above 952 nm, but they have broad absorption in the visible and NIR region and are suitable for the application in DSSCs. It is worth noting that complexes Ru93–Ru94, which contain π-extended ligands, are very weakly soluble in common organic solvents such as MeCN, MeOH, EtOH, and CH2Cl2 and only partially soluble in DMF and DMSO for the measurements of nuclear magnetic resonance, cyclic voltammetry, and electronic spectral properties. Therefore, the problem of solubility must be carefully considered when adopting the π-extended ligands. Fig. 45  Chemical structures of some NIR phosphorescent Ru(II) complexes (Ru93–Ru99). Table 4 lists the photophysical properties of some NIR phosphorescent Ru(II) complexes at RT. Their rich photophysical properties make them attractive candidates for applications as photosensitisers and emitters in DSSCs and LECs, respectively. To date, one of the “brightest” NIR phosphorescent Ru(II) polypyridine complexes, Ru68, has a ΦP, λem, and τ of 0.07, 693 nm, and 5.5 μs, respectively. Therefore, the classical Ru(II) polypyridine complexes need rebirth as NIR phosphorescent emitter. New emissive Ru(II) complexes with new transitions other than 3MLCT, like Ru11, are eagerly expected.4.5 Os(II) complexesPolypyridine d6 Os(II) complexes possess similar photophysical behavior with d6 Ru(II) complexes and they have been thoroughly investigated since the 1980s.331–334 The relationship between the photophysics of Ru(II) diimine and Os(II) diimine complexes illustrates that the emissive excited state is Os to diimine 3MLCT in character. The luminescence of Os(II) complexes is generally of lower energy than that of the related Ru(II) complexes, in part due to the significantly more negative one-electron oxidation potential of the Os(II) complexes. Moreover, the excited state lifetimes of Os(II) diimines are generally shorter than those of the corresponding Ru(II) complexes, which can be explained by the SOC effect and energy gap law.335 For example, [Os(bpy)3]2+ (Os1) has a longer λem and shorter τ of 740 nm and 49 ns, respectively, compared with [Ru(bpy)3]2+ (λem = 606 nm, τ = 870 ns). However, it was demonstrated that the incorporation of strong π- accepting ligands, such as phosphine, arsine,336–339 or carbonyl,340,341 in combination with polypyridyl ligands can improve the τ and ΦP up to tens of microseconds and 0.76, respectively, in the visible region at RT. In these complexes, the 3MLCT and/or 3π–π* ILCT states are responsible for extended τ and improved ΦP. High-performance OLEDs based on these complexes have also been successfully demonstrated.62,64,3394.5.1 Os(II) complexes with bidentate ligands. Since the emission energy of Os(II) complexes is lower than that of the corresponding Ru(II) complexes, a number of studies have been done to seek Os(II) complexes that absorb throughout the visible (black chromophores) and have NIR emission. Some simple Os (II) diimine complexes, such as Os1,337 [trans-Os(bpy)2(PPh3)2]2+ (Os2), [trans-Os(bpy)2(PPh2Me)2]2+ (Os3), [trans-Os(bpy)2(diars)2]2+ (Os4) [diars = 1,2-bis(dimethylarsino)benzene], [Os(bpy)2(CO)(Cl)]+ (Os5), [Os(bpy)2(MeCN)2]2+ (Os6), [Os(bpy)2(pyz)2]2+ (Os7), [Os(bpy)2(py)2]2+ (Os8), [Os(phen)3]2+ (Os9), [trans-Os(phen)2(PPhMe2)2]2+ (Os10), [Os(phen)2(MeCN)2]2+ (Os11), [Os(phen)2(CO)(Cl)]+ (Os12), [Os(phen)2(py)2]2+ (Os13), and [Os(phen)(py)4]2+ (Os14),337 emit RT phosphorescence in the NIR region (Table 5). Among these complexes, Os2–Os4 and Os10, which possess strong π-acid ligands of phosphine or arsine, show blue-shifted emission bands and higher ΦP values up to 0.10,337 in accordance with the energy gap law. On the other hand, the emission spectra can be red shifted up to 1270 nm via the introduction of carboxyl groups to the bpy ligand (Os15 and Os16),342 phenylethynyl group to the phen ligand (Os17 and Os18),343 π-extended ligands, such as dpb ligand ([Os(bpy)2(dpb)]2+ (Os19))312 and eilatin ligands (Os20–Os24),344–346 or imidazole-4,5-dicarboxylic acid ligand (Os25 and Os26)347 (Fig. 46). Upon the deprotonation of NH and CO2H moieties of Os25, the redox potentials of the Os(II)/Os(III) couple are significantly reduced and in consonance with this, the 3MLCT absorption and emission bands are significantly shifted to higher wavelengths, indicating that Os25 has potentially application as a NIR phosphorescent pH probe (Fig. 47).

Fig. 47  Changes in the emission spectra of Os25 as a function of pH in MeCN–H2O (3:2). Inset: the change of emission intensity from 15 to 2 at 825 nm as a function of pH (adapted from ref. 347). As listed in Table 5, either the ΦP or τ value of these NIR phosphorescent Os(II) complexes (Os1–Os26) is relatively small. Recently, the groups of Chi and Chou have reported some elegant results326,348,349 on neutral NIR phosphorescent Os(II) complexes that exhibit significant photophysical properties for application in luminescence solvatochromism or OLEDs. As shown in Fig. 48, Os27348 bearing two (2-pyridyl)pyrazolate ligands together with one carbonyl and one N-heterocyclic ligand exhibits a strong solvent-polarity-dependent phosphorescence, being shifted for example, from 603 nm (in hexane) to 710 nm (in MeCN). This solvatochromic shift is coincident with the phosphorescence originating from a 3LLCT transition. With the π-extended pytriz ligands, Os28–Os31349 emit significantly stronger lower-energy phosphorescence (3MLCT/3ILCT/3LLCT, ΦP and τ up to 0.15 and 760 ns, respectively) than Os27 and the analogous Ru(II) complexes of Ru29 and Ru30. In sharp contrast to the cis-arranged complexes Os29–Os31, the trans derivative Os28, which shows a planar arrangement of chromophoric N-substituted chelates, offers the most effective extended π-delocalization and hence the lowest excited state energy gap. The Os28-based OLEDs exhibit fairly intense emission with a peak wavelength at 814 nm and EQE of 1.5%. Upon switching to Os31, the EL blue shifts to 718 nm, while the maximum EQE increases to 2.7%. Fig. 48  Chemical structures of some NIR phosphorescent Os(II) complexes (Os27–Os31). 4.5.2 Os(II) complexes with terdentate ligands. It is believed that the MC excited states of Os(II) polypyridine complexes lie at relatively higher energy compared with the corresponding Ru(II) polypyridine complexes. This situation is seen clearly for the metal bis-tpy complexes because [Ru(tpy)2]2+ is nonluminescent but [Os(tpy)2]2+ (Os32)350,351 (Fig. 49) emits with reasonable efficiency in the absence of molecular oxygen at RT. A further advantage of the Os(II) complexes containing terdentate ligands (Os32–Os38) is the availability of relatively strong absorption bands stretching across the NIR region. This latter property is useful for the design of luminescent biolabels and PVCs. For instance, a well known Ru(II) complex of [Ru(bpy)2(dppz)]2+ shows no photoluminescence in aqueous solution at ambient temperature, but displays intense photoluminescence in the presence of double-helical DNA, to which the complex binds avidly.352 A similar nonemissive Os(II) complexes of [Os(phen)2(dppz)]2+ can intercalate into DNA and be used as “DNA NIR light switch”,353 as shown in Fig. 50. With π-extended ligands, Os36354 exhibits a broad absorption in the 550–750 nm region and a relatively strong emission band centered at 800 nm (τ = 50 ns and ΦP = 8 × 10−3). The more important fact is that this complex can be completely quenched by hydrogen bonding in aqueous solution but re-display NIR emission in the presence of a small amount of DNA, behaving as a real Off–On DNA light switch (Fig. 50). Although their emission is relatively weak, Os37 and Os38355 exhibit broader absorption in the visible and NIR regions than their analogous Ru(II) complexes and can act as dyes in DSSCs to achieve an overall conversion efficiency up to 8.9%. Fig. 49  Chemical structures of some NIR phosphorescent Os(II) complexes (Os32–Os38).  Fig. 50  Emission spectra of [Os(phen)2(dppz)]2+ and Os36 in buffered solution upon successive addition of DNA (adapted from ref. 353 and 354). 4.5.3 Multinuclear Os(II) complexesA significant amount of work has been done on multinuclear or heteronuclear Os(II) imine complexes with a variety of bridging ligands (BL). These complexes are often reported along with mixed metal Ru(II)(BL)Os(II) systems in which energy transfer from the Ru(II) imine complex to the Os(II) complex is examined.335,356 A typical example is from the work of Juris et al. in which a set of complexes (Os39 and Os40)357 (Fig. 51) of this type were prepared using spirohexane linked bis-phenanthrolines as bridging ligands. In these heteronuclear complexes, since efficient intramolecular triplet energy transfer takes place from the Ru(II)-based fragment (λem = ∼615 nm) to the appended Os(II)-based unit (λem = ∼733 nm), only NIR emission from the latter is observed. However, these multinuclear or heteronuclear Ru(II) complexes (Os23, Os24, Os39 and Os40) generally have relatively low ΦP356 and are out of the focus of this review. Fig. 51  Chemical structures of some NIR phosphorescent Os(II) complexes (Os39 and Os40). The emission from Os(II) complexes is generally at lower energy than that from the analogous Ru(II) species, which is conducive to their use as NIR luminophores. As listed in Table 5, to date, the best ΦP of NIR phosphorescent Os(II) complexes can be up to 0.15, but is difficult for τ to exceed 800 ns, corresponding with Fig. 9. Several efforts have been described to achieve longer τ values for Os(II) complexes. Recently, Lemmetyinen and coworkers demonstrated that Os41358 (Fig. 52) containing a triarylamine donor/9,10-anthraquinone acceptor DA system shows a lengthened lifetime of the charge-separated state (τCR) from ∼50 ns in CH2Cl2 to ∼2 μs in hexafluoroisopropanol (a result of hydrogen bonding). Strong hydrogen bonding between the alcoholic solvent and the reduced anthraquinone causes a thermodynamic and kinetic stabilization of these charge-separated states, resulting in a relatively long τCR. This might provide another tactic to achieve long-lived triplet excited states for Os(II) complexes, however, the τCR in this article is not measured by emission decay but transient absorption. Fig. 52  Chemical structure and mechanism of extended τCR for NIR phosphorescent Os(II) complex of Os41 (adapted from ref. 358). 4.6 Ir(III) complexesToday, d6 octahedral Ir(III) complexes containing cyclometalated ligands such as ppy and its derivatives are the most efficient and versatile class of phosphorescent emitters for use in OLEDs, LECs, TTAU, and energy transfer46,70,71,128,359e.g., they feature shorter triplet lifetimes and less triplet–triplet annihilation at high currents compared to Pt(II) species. As a consequence high quantum efficiencies up to ∼100% can be achieved. Strong SOC leads to mixed singlet and triplet MLCT states as well as to mixed triplet ILCT emitting states, which enables an efficient tuning of the emission color by varying the ligands. Accordingly, full-color applications based on phosphorescent Ir(III) complexes can be realized. As mentioned in Section 3.1.2, ppy-based Ir(III) complexes have much stronger emission than bpy-based Ir(III) complexes. Typically, [Ir(ppy)3] (λem = 492 nm, ΦP = 0.40, τ = 3.6 μs)106 emits much stronger 3MLCT emission of than [Ir(bpy)3]3+(λem = 441 nm, τ = 2.4 μs) [The ΦP of Ir(ppy)3 was recently found to be 0.96 and 0.90 in PMMA and CH2Cl2, respectively.56,188] The ΦP of the latter can be further improved by incorporating both ppy- and bpy-type ligands, resulting in highly emissive and color-tunable [Ir(ppy)2(bpy)]+-type ionic transition-metal complexes (Fig. 12) for the LECs applications.46,1284.6.1 Ir(III) complexes with bidentate ligands. For Ir(III) complexes, it has been demonstrated that expanding the length of π-conjugation in the C⁁N ligand is a much more effective means to lower the emission energy than the effect of substituents and heterocycles in C⁁N ligand. As shown in Fig. 53 and Table 6, the emission energy of the well known ppy-based Ir(III) complexes of [Ir(C⁁N)3] and [Ir(C⁁N)2(acac)] can be lowered into the NIR region(Ir1–Ir10)43,360–363 by expanding the length of π-conjugation in the C⁁N ligands. Especially, Ir743 with the rigid azaperylene ligand gives a marked red shift, emitting at 932 nm. This emission energy is very close to the phosphorescent emission from perylene (800 nm), suggesting that the excited state in this complex is predominantly 3ILCT. Nonetheless, the OLEDs based on these Ir(III) complexes have too low EQE (<1%) for real applications.360–363 Fig. 53  Chemical structures of some NIR phosphorescent Ir(III) complexes (Ir1–Ir10).

For example, dipyrrin and 8-hydroxyquinoline ligands usually have low triplet energy, consequently resulting in their Ir(III) (Ir11–Ir16 and Ir22)364,366 or Pt(II) complexes370 emitting NIR phosphorescence from 3ILCT. Ir17–Ir21 containing π-extended benzoisoquinoline ligands have similar absorption and emission spectra even with different ancillary ligands such as pypz, acac, and bpy, indicating that their transition mainly involves benzoisoquinoline 3π–π* ILCT and metal (Ir) to benzoisoquinoline charge transfer.365 For complex Ir23, a strong red-shifted emission, compared to that of the parent complex [Ir(ppy)2bpy]+ (λem = 580 nm), can be observed at λem = 687 nm; this is attributed to the stabilization of the LUMO by the introduction of the electron withdrawing ester groups onto the bipyridine moiety. The absorption and emission spectra of complex Ir23 and polymer Ir24 are practically the same, indicating that the polymer backbone does not influence the photophysical properties. Moreover, Ir24-based LECs have improved stability and longer emission wavelengths than complex Ir23-based LECs, which can be attributed to a more uniform distribution of the active ionic transition-metal complexes imposed by the chemical linkage to the polymer backbone.367 Like Re23–Re25,285Ir25 containing tricarbonyl diimine complex was found to be capable of binding and detecting F− ions with spectral changes in the absorption and emission (3LLCT transition from Mes2B groups to N⁁N ligand).368 By employing sp2-hybridized N ligand opposite to the chelating N atom in the cyclometalated ligand, Ir27 (λem = 755 nm) has a significantly lower LUMO level than Ir26 (λem = 694 nm), leading to a 60–80 nm red shift in emission of the former relative to the latter.369 Fig. 54  Chemical structures of some NIR phosphorescent Ir(III) complexes (Ir11–Ir27). 4.6.2 Ir(III) complexes with terdentate ligands. [Ir(N⁁N⁁N)2]3+ complexes with terdentate ligands, such as [Ir(tpy)2]3+ (λem = 458 nm, ΦP = 0.025, τ = 1.2 μs),188 generally have low ΦP, but their photophysical properties can be improved by replacing the N⁁N⁁N ligand with N⁁C⁁N variants. For instance, the ΦP of [Ir(dpyx)(ppy)(Cl)] [dpyx = 1,3-di(2-pyridyl)-4,6-dimethylbenzene] (λem = 508 nm, τ = 1.6 μs)371 and [Ir(Phbib)(ppy)(Cl)] [Phbib = bis-(N-phenylbenzimidazolyl)benzene] (λem = 562 nm, τ = 1.55 μs)372 can be enhanced up to 0.75, and 0.95, respectively. Furthermore, replacing N⁁N⁁N ligands with C⁁C⁁N or C⁁N⁁C ligands can lower the emission energy of Ir(III) complexes (Ir28–Ir34)373,374 into the NIR region, as shown in Fig. 55 and Table 6. Despite low luminescent intensity, Ir33 and Ir34 containing C⁁C⁁N ligands show red-shifted phosphorescence [3LLCT(C⁁C⁁N → bpy or phbpy)/MLCT] compared to the [Ir(N⁁N⁁N)(C⁁N⁁C)]+ complexes of Ir28–Ir32 [3LLCT(C⁁N⁁C → N⁁N⁁N)], due to the high π*-orbital energy of C⁁C⁁N ligands. Fig. 55  Chemical structures of some NIR phosphorescent Ir(III) complexes (Ir28–Ir39). 4.6.3 Ir(III) complexes with tetradentate ligands. Compared with Ir(III) complexes with terdentate ligand, luminescent Ir(III) complexes with tetradentate ligand are scarcer, even for Ir(III) porphyrin complexes. This is not surprising, in view of the fact that the Ir(III) porphyrin complexes exhibit distorted octahedral geometry, and that the synthesis and chemistry of such complexes are more difficult than those of the corresponding Pt(II) and Pd(II) metalloporphyrins, which are known to be strongly phosphorescent at RT. Recent work of Borisov et al., however, reveals that Ir(III) octaethylporphyrin (OEP) complex of [Ir(OEP)(CO)(Cl)] Ir35375 (Fig. 55) exhibits strong 3ILCT phosphorescence (λem = 672 nm) at RT with a ΦP and τ of 0.14 and 97 μs, respectively. By replacing the axial CO and Cl ligands with lipophilic ligands such as pyridine or N-(n-butyl)imidazole, the resulting Ir(III) complexes are more soluble and blue-shift emission (λem = 655 nm, ΦP = ∼0.20) and can be used as a water-soluble oxygen probe (by staining bovine serum albumin) and a trace oxygen sensor (by coupling to amino-modified silica gel). Ir36 bearing a π-extended benzoporphyrin ligand emits strong NIR phosphorescence (λem = ∼765 nm) with a ΦP up to 0.30. Unlike Ir(III) porphyrin complexes, Ir(III) corrole complexes (Ir37–Ir39)376 are found to have much weaker NIR phosphorescence (λem = ∼790 nm, ΦP = 0.012–3.3 × 10−4), which might be explained by the lower energy gap between the triplet excited state and the ground state for the metal-free corroles (λem = 790 nm). Moreover, the smaller cavity size of the corrols may result in non-planarity of the Ir(III) corrole complexes, which could promote nonradiative deactivation.4.6.4 Polynuclear Ir(III) complexes. In addition to Ir(III) centers, polynuclear complexes containing either Ru(II), Os(II), Re(II) or Cu(I) polyimine units linked by different bridging ligands display a rich variety of photoinduced energy transfer processes.70 As shown in Fig. 56, a dinuclear complex [(tpy)Ru(py4pyz)Ir(Cl)3]2+ (Ir40)377 [py4pyz = 2,3,5,6-tetrakis(2-pyridyl)pyrazine] incorporating Ru(II) and Ir(III) centers is linked via the bridging ligand of py4pyz. The lowest energy absorption in the electronic spectrum of the bimetallic complex, is an iridium-based 3MLCT transition, while, irrespective of the excited moiety, the emissive state (λem = 810 nm,) appears to be a ruthenium-based 3metal-to-bridging ligand charge-transfer (MBLCT) state. This is consistent with Ir → Ru energy transfer because [Ir(tpy)(Cl)3] and [Ru(tpy)2]2+ both emit from 3MLCT excited states with a λem at 632 nm (τ = 1.31 μs) and 670 nm (τ = 30 ns), respectively. On the contrary, [(bpy)2Ru(HAT)Ir(ppy)2]3+ (Ir41)378 (HAT = 1,4,5,8,9,12-hexaazatriphenylene) linked via the bridging HAT ligand exhibits Ir-based emission (λem = 760 nm, τ < 10 ns), consistent with Ru → Ir energy transfer ([Ir(ppy)2(HAT)]+: λem = 716 nm, τ < 10 ns; [Ru(bpy)2(HAT)]2+: λem = 676 nm, τ = 470 ns). Since the electrochemical data indicate that the closely lying Ir–C σ-bonding and Ir dπ orbitals are located above the Ru-centered dπ level, the emission of Ir41 is attributed to a strong admixture of Ir → HAT 3σ-bond to ligand charge transfer (SBLCT) and 3MLCT behavior. Further examples of tetranuclear heterometallic complexes (Ir42 and Ir43)379 reported by S. Serroni et al. demonstrate that the process of energy transfer in these polynuclear complexes is not only affected by the ligands but also by metal centers. For [M((23dpp)Ir(ppy)2)3]5+ [23dpp = 2,3-bis(2-pyridyl)-pyrazine] with M = Ru(II) or Os(II), the direction of the energy flow is reversed depending on the nature of the central metals. For [Ru((dpp)Ir(ppy)2)3]5+ (Ir42) (λem = 812 nm, ΦP = 1.5 × 10−4, τ = 2.2 ns), the central position is occupied by a [Ru(23dpp)3]2+ unit and the photon absorption of this chromophore results in efficient energy transfer to any one of the peripheral [Ir(dpp)(ppy)2]+ fragments, which emits. Conversely, for [Os((23dpp)Ir(ppy)2)3]5+ (Ir43) (λem = 825 nm, ΦP = 4.5 × 10−4, τ = 5.2 ns), the absorption by one of the Ir(III) chromophores is followed by a periphery-to-center excitation energy, and the emission is [Os(23dpp)3]2+-based. These results agree with the known ordering of the emissive triplet state energy levels: [Ru(23dpp)3]2+ > [Ir(dpp)(ppy)2]+ > [Os(23dpp)3]2+. Even though the study of photoinduced processes in Ir(III)-based arrays has attracted much attention, the resulting complexes usually exhibit low quantum yields (∼10−4),70,188 which limits their applications as NIR luminophores. Fig. 56  Chemical structures of some NIR phosphorescent Ir(III) complexes (Ir40–Ir43). Ir(III) ppy-type complexes, as the most efficient and versatile class of phosphorescent emitters for the applications in OLEDs and LECs, have attracted enormous attention. However, as NIR luminophores, they are no longer the “bright stars”, as listed in Table 6. Fortunately, Ir(III) porphyrin complex Ir36 emits unexpectedly strong NIR phosphorescence (λem = ∼765 nm) with a ΦP up to 0.30, indicating the bright future of Ir(III) complexes as NIR emitters.4.7 Pt(II) complexesAlthough fewer d8 Pt(II) complexes are known to be emissive in fluid solution at RT compared to the d6 metal ions complexes already discussed, Pt(II) complexes with the 4-coordinate square-planar geometry and strong Pt–Pt interactions have uniquely different photophysical properties from the other metal ions, such as Cr(III), Ru(II), Os(II), and Ir(III) with 6-coordinated octahedral geometry as well as Cu(I) with 4-coordinate tetrahedral geometry. The square-planar conformation is responsible for many of the key features that characterize the absorption, luminescence and other excited state properties of Pt(II) complexes. Over the past decades, several classes of phosphorescent Pt(II) complexes with high ΦP, color tunability, and thermal stability have been explored for high-performance OLEDs.65–694.7.1 Pt(II) complexes with bidentate ligands. [Pt(bpy)2]2+ and [Pt(bpy)(Cl)2] are not emissive at RT,98 as discussed in Section 2.1. Replacing bpy or Cl ligand with a ppy or acetylide ligand leads to a strong ligand field and results in strong phosphorescence emission in solution at RT. As examples, [Pt(ppy)(bpy)]+, [Pt(thpy)2],37,68 and [Pt(5,5Me2bpy)(CCPh)2]105 are highly luminescent at RT. A series of [Pt(bpy)(CCPh)2]-type complexes (Pt1–Pt7)380–384 (Fig. 57), reported by Chen et al., usually showed yellow–red emission in solution or in the solid state at RT, and these solid-state samples also could exhibit remarkable luminescence vapochrom and mechanochrom or thermo-triggered luminescence changes into the NIR region (Table 7 and Fig. 58). For instance, solid-state Pt6383 is selectively sensitive to vapors of oxygen-containing volatile organic compounds (VOCs), such as tetrahydrofuran (THF), dioxane, and tetrahydropyrane (THP), and it displays phosphorescence vapochrom change from 561 and 608 nm (3MLCT/LLCT) to 698 nm (toward THF vapor), 689 nm (toward dioxane vapor), and 715 nm (toward THP vapor) (3MMLCT/LLCT), respectively. Crystallographic analysis revealed that the short and long intermolecular Pt–Pt interactions in Pt6·THF crystals are 3.255 Å and 4.646 Å, respectively, while, for Pt6·CH2Cl2 crystals, those change into 4.262 and 4.482 Å, respectively, resulting in Pt6 exhibiting luminescence vapochrom toward THF vapor but not toward CH2Cl2 vapor. Therefore, the variation in the intermolecular Pt–Pt interactions is the key factor in inducing an intriguing luminescence switch. Upon mechanically grinding, desolvated Pt6 exhibits significant mechanoluminescence red shifts from 561 and 608 nm to 730 nm. Moreover, when Pt6 is heated, a thermo-triggered luminescence change occurs, leading to red shifting the bright yellow luminescence at 561 and 608 nm into red luminescence at 667 nm, as shown in Fig. 58. It is interesting, even though Pt3–Pt5382 have similar chemical structures, that they exhibit totally different luminescence vapochromic properties. Pt3 does not exhibit any vapoluminescence to common VOC vapors, but Pt4 and Pt5 have distinct vapoluminescence toward CHCl3 and CHCl3/CH2Cl2 vapors, respectively. In addition to intermolecular Pt–Pt interactions, –SiMe3 groups, π–π interactions, host–guest interactions, and hydrogen bonds also play important roles for vapoluminescence. It was demonstrated that the addition of –CO2Et group to the bpy ligand (Pt8),385 the introduction of π-extended systems to the acetylide ligand (Pt9–Pt13),66,386,387 or use of the strongly π-accepting bis(mesitylimino)acenaphthene ligands (Pt14–Pt18)388 (Fig. 57) are all efficient methods to lower the emission energy into the NIR region. It is noteworthy that Pt8–Pt10 complexes containing π-extended ligands exhibit dual fluorescence/phosphorescence emission, as mentioned in Section 2.3.2. Pt13,387 reported by Zhao et al., has an exceptionally long lived T1 excited state (54.7 μs, measured by transient absorption) and can be used as a triplet sensitizer for TTAU with an upconversion quantum yield of 22.4%, in stark contrast to the previously reported upconversion quantum yields for the transition-metal complexes of less than 20%. Fig. 57  Chemical structures of some NIR phosphorescent Pt(II) complexes (Pt1–Pt18).

nsILCT432Pt139Toluene65710291.2 × 10−320.1 nsILCT432Pt140Toluene65410032.7 × 10−325.5 nsILCT432Pt141MeCN5457801.5 × 10−3—LLCT/ILCT/MLCT458Pt142MeCN5507841.4 × 10−3—LLCT/ILCT/MLCT458 Fig. 58  Solid-state emission spectra of crystalline and corresponding ground materials based on complex Pt6 VOC at ambient temperature (adapted from ref. 383). Another type of NIR phosphorescent diimine-based Pt(II) complexes are the [Pt(diimine)(dithiolate)] complexes (Pt19–Pt28),112,389,390 that were explored by Eisenberg and shown in Fig. 59. In these heteroleptic complexes where 3LL′CT (L = dithiolate ligand, L′ = diimine ligand) excited states have been proposed, it is likely that the 3ML′CT excited states should be involved too. Even though most of them have low ΦP (10−3–10−5) at RT, these [Pt(diimine)(dithiolate)] complexes with strong absorption in the visible and NIR regions (Fig. 60) can be used as sensitizers for DSSCs.390 Fig. 59  Chemical structures of some NIR phosphorescent Pt(II) complexes (Pt19–Pt29).  Fig. 60  Normalized absorption (in CH2Cl2) and emission spectra (in frozen butyronitrile at 77 K) of some [Pt(diimine)(dithiolate)] complexes (adapted from ref. 112). In addition to diimine ligands, 1,2-enedithiolates (Pt30–Pt34),138,391,392 8-hydroxyquinoline/quinoline-8-thiol (Pt35–Pt46),370,393,394 dipyrrin (Pt47),395 or C⁁N ligands containing π-extended systems (Pt48–Pt53)140,141,396 are employed to prepare NIR phosphorescent Pt(II) complexes, as depicted in Fig. 61. Like Ir11–Ir16 and Ir22, Pt35–Pt46 containing dipyrrin and 8-hydroxyquinolin/quinoline-8-thiol ligands emit NIR phosphorescence from 3ILCT. As discussed in Section 2.3.2, Pt48–Pt53, like Pt8–Pt10, containing π-extended ligands show dual fluorescence/phosphorescence emission (Fig. 15 and Fig. 62), which might reduce T1 → S0 and result in low ΦP. Thus, it is obvious that the approach of simply extending the conjugation in order to obtain more red-shifted emission starts to break down. This is particularly significant for the design of technologically relevant NIR phosphorescent complexes, especially for Pt(II) and Os(II) complexes with low efficiency of SOC, because Ir(III) complexes containing similar ligands with Pt48 and Pt49 have strong SOC and show phosphorescence, but no fluorescence.140 However, dual fluorescent/phosphorescent molecular luminophores are scarce from a molecular photophysical perspective because a delicate balance between the S1 and T1 excited states must be kept. Non-efficient ISC will lead to fluorescence; as a result, no phosphorescence can be observed. Conversely, an efficient ISC will eliminate the fluorescence and only phosphorescence can be observed. Only those luminophores with moderate ISC effects can display the dual fluorescence and phosphorescence emission.141 These dual fluorescent/phosphorescent materials have some useful applications such as ratiometric optical oxygen sensing,38 as shown in Fig. 62. Pt30–Pt34 with 1,2-enedithiolates ligands also exhibit dual fluorescent/phosphorescent emission when protonated or deprotonated. Fig. 61  Chemical structures of some NIR phosphorescent Pt(II) complexes (Pt30–Pt53).  Fig. 62  Ratiometric luminescence response of Pt52 and Pt53 to variation of the O2 concentration (0.000%, 0.201%, 0.998%, 1.501%, 3.5%, 5.0%, 10.0%, 20.0% O2, v/v) in toluene (adapted from ref. 141). 4.7.2 Pt(II) complexes with terdentate ligands. Unlike its rich luminescence properties exhibited in low-temperature glass media, the parent Pt(II) terpyridyl complex, [Pt(tpy)(Cl)]+, is found to be nonemissive in solution at RT due to the presence of lower-lying or thermally accessible 3d–d excited states, which provide facile deactivation pathways via molecular distortion. However, the emission of solid-state [Pt(tpy)(Cl)]+ at RT is broad, structure-less, and occurs at relatively long wavelengths with the characteristics indicative of charge-transfer emission from a polypyridine complex of platinum involved in neighbor–neighbor interactions. As shown in Fig. 63, two complex cations stack in the unit cell in a head-to-tail orientation with an intermolecular Pt–Pt separation of 3.329 Å.397 Strong d(z2)–d(z2) interactions result in emission from 3dσ*π* states, often designated as 3MMLCT states.398–400 Through aryl-modified/fused terpyridine ligands or the replacement of the chloro auxiliary ligand with stronger-field ligands, such as alkynyl or –CN group, the photophysical properties of Pt(II) tpy-typed complexes can be dramatically improved.398 For instance, [Pt(4CN-tpy)(CN)]+ (4Me2N-tpy = 4′-dimethylamino-2,2′:6′,2′′-terpyridine) exhibits a low-energy charge-transfer excitation at λem = 520 nm with ΦP = 0.26 and τ = 22 μs in CH2Cl2 at RT.401 Fig. 63  A perspective drawing of the [Pt(tpy)(Cl)]+ cation (adapted from ref. 397). As shown in Fig. 64, Yam et al. whose work focuses on luminescent alkynyl complexes,261,283,284 firstly reported a series of luminescent Pt(II) terpyridyl acetylide complexes, [Pt(tpy)(CCR)]+ (Pt54–Pt62),402–408 with enhanced solubility and intense emission in solution due to the presence of the alkynyl group. In general, Pt54–Pt57402 in dilute MeCN at RT show orange–red phosphorescence (3MLCT) (λem = 560–665 nm, ΦP = 1.1 × 103–0.012, τ ≤ 500 ns), originating from the [dπ(Pt) → π*(tpy)] 3MLCT excited state with some mixing of a [π(CCR) → π*(tpy)] 3LLCT character. However, upon going from MeCN solutions to the solid state, the lower-energy emission bands (3MMLCT) are observed at 710–800 nm at RT (Table 7). Further work demonstrates that recrystallization of [Pt59]OTf,403 under different conditions afforded two crystal forms, a dark green form and a red form. As shown in Fig. 65, the complex cations are equally separated with short intermolecular Pt–Pt contacts of 3.388 Å in the dark green crystal form, while in the red crystal form, a zigzag arrangement of pairs of complex cations showing a dimeric structure with alternating short and long Pt–Pt distances of 3.394 and 3.648 Å, respectively, is observed. The difference in the crystal colors has been attributed to the variations of the intermolecular arrangement as well as the different extent of Pt–Pt and π–π stacking interactions due to aggregation.400 Dissolution of either of the two crystal forms gives a yellow solution in MeCN, but upon adding the insoluble solvent of diethyl ether (EA) to MeCN, the solution of aggregated Pt59 exhibits “solvatochromism” at 615 nm and “solvatoluminescence” at 785 nm (Fig. 65).403 The photophysical properties in these solvent-induced self-assembly aggregates of [Pt59]X (X = OTf−, PF6−, ClO4−, BF4−) also were found to be dependent on the nature of the anions (Fig. 65), while no observable color or spectral changes were found for the larger non-coordinating BPh4− anion.404 In light of the fact that the counter-anions play an important role in governing the assembly of the positively charged [Pt(tpy)(CCR)]+ complex cations, a polyelectrolyte, namely poly(acrylic acid), upon deprotonation can indeed induce self-assembly of Pt59, and, in turn, induce remarkable UV/Visible and NIR emission intensity changes,405,407 as shown in Fig. 66. Moreover, single-stranded nucleic acids, which carry multiple negative charges in an aqueous medium at near neutral pH, also induce the aggregation and self-assembly of Pt59 and Pt60via electrostatic binding of the platinum complexes to the single-stranded nucleic acids.406 Therefore, it is anticipated that such drastic solvent-induced sensitive changes in color and on–off switching of emission via aggregation of metal–metal-bonded and π-stacked species will serve as versatile reporters and probes of environmental changes and may find potential applications in materials science and sensor technology. Subsequently, a “proof-of-principle” study illustrates that the self-assembly NIR emissive lysozyme aptamer (LA)- or thrombin-binding aptamer (TBA)–Pt61 mixture408 are sensitive towards the conformational and microenvironmental changes of aptamers and can be used as label- and immobilization-free turn-off probes for lysozyme and thrombin detection with good selectivity and specificity, since that lysozyme and thrombin have higher binding affinity with aptamers than Pt61 (Fig. 67). Without any aggregation effect, Pt64–Pt68,409–412 which contain π-extended systems in the N⁁N⁁N ligands, emit NIR emission in dilute solution at RT. Fig. 64  Chemical structures of some NIR phosphorescent Pt(II) complexes (Pt54–Pt68). (b) Photographs of [Pt59]X (X = Otf−, PF6−, ClO4−, BF4−) in a solution of MeCN–EA mixture upon solvent-induced aggregation. The EA content from left to right: 86%, 86%, 82%, 82% (adapted from ref. 404). (b) (left) Electronic absorption spectral changes of Pt59 (0.15 mM) in a mixture of MeOH and MeCN (2:1, v/v) upon addition of poly(acrylic acid) (0.45 mM carboxylic acid unit) and TBAH (0.45 mM). Inset: photographs showing the corresponding color change. (right) NIR luminescence enhancement of Pt59 upon addition of polyacrylate with equimolar amounts of carboxylic acid and base (adapted from ref. 405).  Fig. 67  (a) Schematic cartoon showing the disassembly of Pt61 from LA upon lysozyme binding. (b) Schematic cartoon showing the disassembly of Pt61 from TBA upon thrombin binding. (c) Emission spectral changes of Pt61 upon addition of various concentrations of lysozyme to LA-complex mixture with 2 mM of LA and 42 mM of Pt61 (left) and thrombin to TBA–hemin-complex mixture with 6 mM of TBA, 6 mM of hemin and 42 mM of Pt61 (right) in respective buffer conditions (adapted from ref. 408). One of the potential advantages of Pt(II) complexes containing a terdentate ligand over a bidentate ligand relates to the inhibition of the D2d distortion that bis-bidentate complexes can undergo, through twisting of the two planes relative to one another. Thus, if the cyclometallating ring could be incorporated into a terdentate ligand rather than a bidentate ligand (e.g., C⁁N⁁N rather than C⁁N), then further increases in the luminescence efficiency of Pt(II) complexes [e.g., [Pt(phbpy)(Cl)] (λem = 565 nm, ΦP = 0.025, τ = 510 ns in dilute CH2Cl2)]104 might be anticipated.69 Che and co-workers have pioneered413 and done much work on luminescent C⁁N⁁N-based Pt(II) complexes (ΦP up to 0.68 in CH2Cl2)414 and their applications (Fig. 11, 16, and 19).43,104,129,152,154,413–415 For instance, Pt69–Pt71152,413 (Fig. 68) are highly orange (3MLCT) emitters (λem = 550–566 nm, ΦP = 0.21–0.057, τ = 0.63–14 μs) in dilute CH2Cl2 at RT. In the aggregated state, they have short Pt–Pt contacts, e.g., 3.37 Å for Pt69, resulting in red-shifted NIR emission (3MMLCT) (Table 7), like Pt54–Pt62. With the covalent attachment of biocompatible poly(ethylene glycol) (PEG), polymer Pt72415 yields dual emission from the monomer (λem = 543 nm, 3MLCT) and aggregation (λem = 710 nm, 3MMLCT) of the [Pt(C⁁N⁁N)(Cl)] moieties in water. Since PEG polymer behaves like a typical non-ionic surfactant in water, micellar assemblies can form with the hydrophobic [Pt(C⁁N⁁N)(Cl)] moieties embedded inside, leading to aggregation of the [Pt(C⁁N⁁N)(Cl)] moieties. The emission of Pt72 is enhanced in hydrophobic regions of protein molecules in aqueous solution, thus Pt72 can function as a luminescent probe for protein denaturation due to the urea unfolding reaction, as demonstrated in Fig. 69. Intermolecular hydrogen bonds in Pt73416 also are found to be facile inducers of strong Pt–Pt interactions (3.3–3.4 Å) in the solid state, resulting in the NIR emission from Pt73 excimers, as shown Fig. 70. Fig. 68  Chemical structures of some NIR phosphorescent Pt(II) complexes (Pt69–Pt73).  Fig. 69  Emission spectra of Pt72 (20 mM) in bovine serum albumin (BSA) aqueous solution. From bottom to top, [BSA] (mM): 0.00 to 3.15, step: 0.15. Insert: variation of ratios of luminescence intensity at 710 nm to that at 543 nm vs.Pt72 concentration in aqueous solution (measured at 543 nm, excited at 390 nm, 298 K) (adapted from ref. 415).  Fig. 70  A hydrogen-bonded Pt73 excimer through intermolecular hydrogen bonds between the uracil moieties (adapted from ref. 416). An alternative route to obtain highly luminescent Pt(II) complexes is cyclometalation with N⁁C⁁N-coordinating dpyb ligands, as reported by Williams et al.67–69 Structurally, dpyb ligands lead to shorter Pt–C bonds (1.90 Å) than in the previous isomeric C⁁N⁁N systems (2.04 Å), thus the parent complex [Pt(dpyb)(Cl)] (Pt74)417 (Fig. 71) in dilute CH2Cl2 at RT exhibits strong phosphorescence (λem = 491 nm, ΦP = 0.60, τ = 7.2 μs) with a highly structured profile and a very small Stokes shift, implying a predominant 3π–π* assignment for the emissive state. Like C⁁N⁁N-based Pt(II) complexes Pt69–Pt73, N⁁C⁁N-based Pt(II) complexes Pt74–Pt76167,418 in aggregated states have a new strong NIR emission (ΦP = 0.24–0.35 for vacuum-evaporated neat films) at about 700 nm from the excimers, which can be used as NIR emitters in OLEDs to achieve unusually high EQEs up to 14.5%, as shown in Fig. 72. If doping Pt75 into a host, white OLEDs can be achieved by the simultaneous emission of monomer in blue and excimer in red from a single emitter of Pt75.419 The further work on Pt77–Pt80420 by the same group revealed that the introduction of trifluoromethyl groups onto the N⁁C⁁N ligand leads to a red shift in the excimer emission band (e.g., λem = 756 nm for Pt78), but the introduction of F atoms showed inverse effects (e.g., λem = 677 nm for Pt77), as shown in Fig. 73. In addition to the chemical structures of N⁁C⁁N ligands, the auxiliary ligand X in [Pt(N⁁C⁁N)X] complexes has strong effects on Pt–Pt interactions in aggregated states. Pt81 and Pt82421 with the same N⁁C⁁N ligand and different auxiliary ligand X (X = Cl and NCS for Pt81 and Pt82, respectively) displayed similar luminescence in solution but intensively different luminescence in the solid state, due to their different molecular packing. Pt82 (X = NCS) has much shorter Pt–Pt interactions (3.3 Å) than Pt81 (X = Cl, Pt–Pt interactions = 4.4 Å), leading to a red-shifted EL band up to 855 nm for Pt82 (λEL = 685 nm for Pt81). Like Pt1–Pt7, [Pt(N⁁C⁁N)(Cl)]-typed complex of Pt83422 exhibits the luminescent mechanochromism in the solid state, accounted for by excimer emission. Furthermore, Che,423,424 Yam,425 and colleagues have investigated the photophysics of [Pt(C⁁N⁁C)L] complexes containing the cyclometalated ligand 2,6-diphenylpyridine, denoted as dppy or C⁁N⁁C (Fig. 23). Unfortunately, these complexes containing L ligand of 4-t-butylpyridine, 2,6-diphenylisocyanide, or triphenylphosphine generally display weak-to-no emission in dilute solution and strong red emission in solid state at RT. Fig. 71  Chemical structures of some NIR phosphorescent Pt(II) complexes (Pt74–Pt83).  Fig. 72  (a) Energy level diagram of the NIR EL devices and molecular structures of the materials used. (b) EL spectra recorded at the applied voltage of 10 V (adapted from ref. 167 and 418).  Fig. 73  Emission spectra of the Pt74 and Pt77–Pt80 in CH2Cl2 with high concentration of 1.5 × 10−4 M at 298 K. The weak excimer bands of Pt78–Pt80 have been expanded for clarity (adapted from ref. 420). 4.7.3 Pt(II) complexes with tetradentate ligands. As discussed in Section 3.3, d8 Pt(II) complexes with the 4-coordinate square-planar geometry have inherent advantages in being chelated by tetradentate ligands and result in highly stable and emissive complexes. [Pt(qtpy)]2+ complexes Pt84 and Pt85426 (Fig. 74), reported by Che et al. have similar chemical structures, in which only Pt84 with the steric rigidity from four methyl groups displays strong RT green emission (λem = 496 nm, ΦP = 0.026, τ = 7.0 μs) in dilute MeCN, originated from 3π–π* or 3MLCT. However, in the solid state, both of these complexes show weak NIR emission at about 700 nm. The shortest Pt–Pt distances of 7.249 and 5.904 Å for Pt84 and Pt85, respectively, are too long for any Pt–Pt interaction, suggesting the possibility of having ligand π–π interactions in the solid state. Thus, it seems that significant Pt–Pt interactions in the solid state of Pt(II) polypyridine complexes occur only when either the metal atom is bonded to three or less pyridyl units with the remaining coordination sites occupied by small molecules or when the four coordinating aryl rings bow to one side for strain release. Some other tetradentate ligands, especially N⁁O⁁O⁁N ligands,126,207–209,427 have been used to obtain highly emissive (ΦP up to 0.6) Pt(II) complexes. Among these complexes, Pt86166 and Pt87208 exhibit NIR emission in dilute solution. Fig. 74  Chemical structures of some NIR phosphorescent Pt(II) complexes (Pt84–Pt98). To date, the best successful and known NIR phosphorescent transition-metal complexes are Pt(II) porphyrin complexes due to their highly thermal and chemical stability, good solubility, rich chemical modification and high luminescence. For example, [Pt(OEP)] (Pt88) (Fig. 74) emits strong deep-red phosphorescence (λem = 646 nm, ΦP = 0.5, τ = 100 μs)38 from 3ILCT in solution and has wide applications in OLEDs77 and oxygen sensing.38 The molecules of Pt88 are found to aggregate on the (001) surface of KBr, resulting in NIR emission (λem = 775 nm).428 One possible way to tune phosphorescence of Pt(II) porphyrin complexes is to modify the tetrapyrrole macrocycle of porphyrin ligands. The heart of the porphyrin structure is an 18 π-electron aromatic system, which determines basic optical properties and electronic spectra of the resulting complexes. Hydrogenation of one or more of the exo-pyrrole double bonds in the macrocycle (like that occurring in related structures of chlorins, bacteriochlorins, and dihydrochlorins) results in substantial red shifting of both absorbance and emission spectra. Pt(II) complexes of chlorins, bacteriochlorins, et al. are also known to show phosphorescence, but they are less stable than corresponding porphyrin complexes. Furthermore, it is even difficult to synthesize Pt(II) complexes of the “reduced porphyrins”, since metalation at elevated temperatures is accompanied by the decomposition of such compounds.429 Papkovsky et al. reported a series of Pt(II) porphyrin ketones (or oxochlorins) complexes (Pt89–Pt92)429,430 with improved photochemical and chemical stability and strong NIR phosphorescence (λem = 758–767 nm, ΦP = 0.1–0.12, τ = 24–60 μs) in micellar sulfite solution (MSS) or phosphate buffered saline (PBS) for the application of oxygen sensing and live-cell fluorescence imaging, as shown in Fig. 75. Some other types of tetrapyrrole macrocycle are also used to prepare NIR phosphorescent Pt(II) complexes (Pt93–Pt96).431–433 One successful application is the use of dual luminophor paint containing pressure-sensitive Pt93 and temperature-sensitive [Mg(TFPP)] [TFPP = meso-tetra(pentafluorophenyl)porphyrin], in which the latter provides temperature-sensitive paint as the pressure-independent reference (Fig. 76). The emission intensity ratio of Pt93/[Mg(TFPP)] produced ideal pressure measurements in a wind tunnel with a very low-temperature dependence of –0.1% °C−1.434 As discussed in Section 3.3, as another important class of tetrapyrrole macrocycle, Pc and its complexes with strong π–π stacks are less soluble and emissive than porphyrin. Usually, Pt(II) phthalocyanine complexes (Pt97 and Pt98)237,241,435 display dual fluorescence emission (λem = ∼700 nm) and NIR phosphorescence emission (λem = ∼1000 nm) with low Φ. Fig. 75  Confocal fluorescence image of A549 cells loaded with Pt92 and contained with (a) or without (b) 4,6-diamidino-2-phenylindole (adapted from ref. 430).  Fig. 76  (a) Emission spectra of the dual sensor film at several percentages oxygen concentrations. (b) Pressure measurements (Pt93/[Mg(TFPP)]) in wind tunnel for binary formulation at V =94 m s−1 and three yaw angles: −20°, 0° and 20° (adapted from ref. 434). An alternative way to achieve red-shifted phosphorescence is to extend π-conjugated system of porphyrin core at meso-positions (5, 10, 15, and 20-position) or β-pyrrole positions (2, 3, 7, 8, 12, 13, 17, and 18-position). However, the former has been demonstrated to not be an efficient way to extend the π-conjugated system. For instance, as shown in Fig. 8, [Pt(TPP)] containing four phenyl groups at meso-positions of porphyrin has a similar emission spectrum with [Pt(OEP)] (Pt88) because these phenyl groups in [Pt(TPP)] are almost vertical to the porphyrin core and have little contribution to extending the π-conjugated system. Recent work,436–438 done by Vinogradov and Thompson, respectively, revealed that porphyrin chromophores with fused aromatic moieties at the β-pyrrole positions, for example, tetrabenzoporphyrin (TBP) or tetranaphthaloporphyrins (TNP), exhibit a bathochromic shift of the absorption and emission energy, owing to the expansion of the π-conjugated system of the porphyrin core. Since then, Pt(II) π-extended porphyrin complexes (Pt99–Pt124)433,436–446 (Fig. 77) have received great attention. Pt113437 with the meso-unsubstituted TBP ligand displays strong NIR phosphorescence (λem = 745 nm, ΦP = 0.51, τ = 50 μs) in DMF at RT. The presence of four phenyl groups at meso-positions of TBP in Pt99438 leads to more soluble non-planar porphyrins and further red-shifted absorption and emission spectra (λem = 765 nm, ΦP = 0.7, τ = 53 μs). The fluorinated derivatives (Pt100–Pt102)439,440 of Pt99 show not only improved solubility and photo-stability but also lower energy in emission bands (λem = 773–788 nm, ΦP = 0.27–0.60, τ = 38–50 μs). The electron-withdrawing CO2R groups in Pt104 and Pt105443 further lower their emission bands to 791 nm. Dendritic Pt105 containing the covalent attachment of biocompatible PEG might be used as phosphorescent probes for oxygen imaging in biological systems. [B(dipyrrin)(F)2]–[Pt(TBP)] hybrid complex Pt109444 exhibits broad visible-light absorption, fast and efficient intramolecular energy transfer from the [B(dipyrrin)(F)2] to [Pt(TBP)] unit, and strong NIR phosphorescence (λem = 772 nm, ΦP = 0.26) with an unusually long-lived τ of 69 μs. A study of an extensive set of variously meso-substituted [Pt(TBP)] reveals that the emission spectra of 5,15-diaryl-substituted complexes (Pt110–Pt112)441 generally exhibit blue shifts with longer τ and enhanced ΦP values compared to those of 5,10,15,20-tetraryl-substitued complexes, because the tetraphenyl-substitued complexes are more distorted from planarity, which increases the nonradiative decay rate, shortening τ and ΦP. It is an exception for Pt114441 which exhibits a distinct red-shifted phosphorescence band at 786 nm. At the same time, this study has proved that the best efficient way to lower emission band is further extending π-conjugated system at β-pyrrole positions. As shown in Fig. 78, the Pt(II) tetrabenzo- (Pt99), tetranaphtho- (Pt115), and tetraanthroporphyrin (Pt119)441 complexes exhibit phosphorescence at λem ranging from 770 to 1020 nm. However, it should be noted that the radiative decay rates from the triplet states are relatively constant across the series; on the other hand, the non-radiative decay rate increases substantially with the decreasing T1 → S0 energy gap. An energy gap law correlation was observed, which indicates there is a limited prospect for extending the emission wavelength to longer wavelengths because the ΦP will become very low. Pt120–Pt124445,446 with less symmetry were also explored. Pt120 (λem = 841 nm) and Pt121445 (λem = 844 nm) containing 5,10-biaryl- and 5,10,15-teraryl-TBP, respectively, displayed distinct red-shifted phosphorescence compared to the 5,15-biaryl- (Pt110) (λem = 770 nm) or 5,10,15,20-tetraryl-TBP-based (Pt99) (λem = 765 nm) Pt(II) complex. On the contrary, Pt122–Pt124446 (λem = 815–870 nm) with less π-extended system at β-pyrrole positions give blue-shift shifted phosphorescence compared to Pt115 (λem = 891 nm). These Pt(II) π-extended porphyrin complexes with strong absorption in visible/NIR region, intense NIR phosphorescence, and high stability, have a variety of applications, such as NIR OLEDs,438,442,447,448 PVCs,449,450 TTAUs,451 oxygen sensors,433,440,443,445,446 and biomedical applications.443,452 For example, Pt103-442 and Pt115-based448 OLEDs exhibit intense NIR EL with EQE of 9.2% (λem = 773 nm) and 3.8% (λem = 896 nm), respectively. Pt115, encapsulated into phospholipid nanomicelles, can be used as efficient NIR optical probes for tumor imaging and detection (Fig. 79).452 Fig. 77  Chemical structures of some NIR phosphorescent Pt(II) complexes (Pt99–Pt124).  Fig. 78  Emission spectra of the π-extended Pt(II) porphyrins in toluene (adapted from ref. 441).  Fig. 79  (a) PL images of the tumored nude mouse at various time points (2, 24, 96 h) postinjection with the nanomicelles based on Pt115. (b) Spectral profiles used to un-mix images shown in (a). (c) Bright field and PL images of the major organs resected from mouse 96 h post-injection (adapted from ref. 452). 4.7.4 Polynuclear Pt(II) complexes. Unlike polynuclear Ru(II) or Ir(III) complexes, some polynuclear Pt(II) complexes (Pt125–Pt142)154,155,160,161,432,454–458 (Fig. 80) are highly emissive in the NIR region and have received great attention. As discussed previously, for mononuclear Pt(II) complexes, intermolecular Pt–Pt interactions play an important role in red shifting emission spectrum, while, for polynuclear Pt(II) complexes, e.g.Pt125, Pt126, and Pt129–Pt135, intramolecular Pt–Pt interactions are the keys for NIR emission. In a series of pyrazolate-bridged cyclometalated Pt(II) complexes, such as Pt125,155 the bridging pyrazolate controls the degree of intramolecular Pt–Pt interactions and, thus, the nature of the excited state. The two bulky tert-butyl groups in Pt125 force the two C⁁N Pt moieties closer together, resulting in the shortest Pt–Pt distance (2.8343 Å) and, thus, the most red-shifted emission band (λem = ∼690 nm) in dilute solution at RT, as shown in Fig. 81. Pt126453 displays strong NIR phosphorescence (λem = 721 nm, ΦP = 0.18) from 3MMLCT in solution and is suitable for the application in OLEDs (EQE = 6.3%). The dinuclear alkynylPt(II) terpyridyl complexes of Pt129 and Pt130,160 like the mononuclear alkynylPt(II) terpyridyl complex of Pt59, are found to show inter- and/or intramolecular aggregation in fluid solution state at low temperature or even at RT and, thus, drastic colour changes and NIR emission (3MMLCT) enhancement, which may find useful applications as molecular temperature probes with tunable operation range. However, Pt131160 is an exception and has the negative result from its variable-temperature emission study, suggesting that the incorporation of bulky tert-butyl moieties in the terpyridyl ligand inhibits the self-assembly behaviour of Pt131 and hence the emission originates from an excited state of 3MLCT character which has a higher energy than its analogue Pt130. Pt132,161 the first example of phosphorescent tweezers, shows host–guest interactions with cationic, neutral and anionic square-planar Pt(II), as well as heterometallic Pd(II), Au(I), and Au(III) guests, as supported by spectroscopic measurements in CH2Cl2 solution. The binding affinity between the host and the guest is strongly perturbed by the Pt–M, π–π and electrostatic interactions, giving rise to various spectroscopic changes depending on the nature of the guest molecules. Among a series of dinuclear Pt(II), Au(III), and mixed Pt–Au complexes built on a xanthene scaffold, only dinuclear Pt(II) complex Pt135456 with strong intramolecular Pt–Pt interactions emits NIR emission (λem = 817 nm) in dilute solution at RT. In addition to dinuclear Pt(II) complexes, some ternuclear Pt(II) complexes, such as Pt133 and Pt134,154 are found to display NIR emission in dilute solution at RT. For the red crystal of Pt133, the intramolecular metal–metal contacts of 3.194 Å at Pt1–Pt2 and 3.399 Å at Pt2–Pt3, with the Pt1–Pt2–Pt3 angle being 169°, indicate d8–d8 interactions across Pt1–Pt2–Pt3, resulting in red-shifted emission (λem = 710 nm in MeCN at RT) compared to the analogous dinuclear Pt(II) complex of [(Pt(phbpy))2(μ-dppm)]2+ (Fig. 19) (λem = 652 nm in MeCN at RT) with intramolecular Pt–Pt contacts of 3.270 Å.152 For other dinuclear Pt(II) complexes (Pt127,454Pt128,455 and Pt136–Pt140432,457) in Fig. 80, the two Pt(II) metal centers were found to be separated and, thus, the absence of intramolecular Pt–Pt interactions. For example, the Pt–Pt distances of Pt127 and Pt128 are 4.501 Å and 8.538 Å, respectively, beyond the range of intermetal distances (<3.50 Å) normally observed in monomeric Pt(II) extended linear-chain structures. Thus, their NIR emission is ascribed to 3LLCT (thiolate → diimine) and 3MLCT [dπ(Pt) → π*(tpy)]/3LLCT [π(CC) → π*(tpy)], respectively. Pt128 containing calix[4]crown-5 was been demonstrated to show luminescence enhancement, upon metal ion-binding, with a more selective binding toward K+ over the other alkali-metal and alkaline earth-metal ions (Fig. 82). Since attaching Pt(II) coordination centers directly onto the [B(dipyrrin)(F)2] core in Pt136457 using acetylide bonds, rather than on the periphery of the [B(dipyrrin)(F)2] core maximizes the heavy-atom effect of Pt(II) ion, the ISC is facilitated and the radiative decay of the T1 excited state of [B(dipyrrin)(F)2] is observed, resulting in dual 3MLCT emission at 660 nm and the 3ILCT emission at 770 nm with unusually long τ of 128.4 μs at RT. Some heteronuclear Pt(II) complexes (Pt141 and Pt142)458 were also reported. They usually have low ΦP, which limits their applications as NIR luminophores. Fig. 80  Chemical structures of some NIR phosphorescent Pt(II) complexes (Pt125–Pt142).  Fig. 81  Normalized emission spectra of Pt(II) complexes [bridging pyrazolate = pyrazolate (1), 3,5-dimethylpyrazolate (2), 3-methyl-5-tert-butylpyrazolate (3), and 3,5-bis(tert-butyl)pyrazolate (4 or Pt125); [Pt(46dFppy)(pyrazolate)B(Et)2 (5)] in MeTHF(adapted from ref. 155).  Fig. 82  Absorption and emission spectral changes of Pt128 upon addition of various concentrations of KPF6 and photograph showing the effect of various metal ions (5.0 × 10−5 M) on the emission intensities of Pt128 (3.7 × 10−5 M) in MeCN (adapted from ref. 455). Table 7 summarizes the photophysical properties of some NIR phosphorescent Pt(II) complexes at RT. Among the NIR transition-metal complexes, Pt(II) complexes, especially Pt(II) porphyrin complexes, exhibit the best photophysical properties including long τ, high ΦP, tunable emission bands, and wide applications. Pt(II) complexes with the 4-coordinate square-planar geometry have inherent advantages in being chelated by π-extended tetradentate ligands to achieve highly strong NIR phosphorescence, therefore, new tetradentate ligands rather than porphyrins are expectant. Furthermore, recent studies reveal that Pt(III)459 and Pt(IV)460,461 complexes are luminescent as well, which might be a new direction for the design of luminescent Pt complexes.4.8 Pd(II) complexesThe heavy atom effect exerted by the d8 Pd(II) metal centre is much weaker than that of Pt(II), and consequently phosphorescent Pd(II) complexes are rare when compared with the analogous Pt(II) complexes.37 Most Pd(II) complexes display weak triplet emission only at low temperature, with RT emission generally restricted to fluorescence. It also has been recognized that when incorporated into polynuclear complexes, Pd(II) centers may lead to partial quenching of luminescence.462,463 Despite these unfavorable premises, luminescent cyclometalated Pd(II) complexes have been designed for applications in OLEDs463–465 and oxygen sensing.429,433,436,439,440,443,445,446,466,467 In particular, Pd(II) porphyrin complexes (Pd1–Pd30)429,433,436,437,440,443,445,446,465–467 (Fig. 83) display intense NIR phosphorescence, but with significantly low ΦP and long τ, and red-shifted emission bands compared with the analogous Pt(II) complexes, as listed in Table 8. For instance, Pd10440 is a dual emitter with strong NIR phosphorescence and weak red fluorescence at RT.468 The λem, ΦP, and τ of Pd10 is 800 nm, 0.21, and 286 μs,440 respectively, while that of its analogous Pt99 is 765 nm, 0.7, and 53 μs,438 respectively. However, it is noteworthy that the metalation of Pd(II) ions into porphyrin ligands is usually easier than that of Pt(II) ions. Fig. 83  Chemical structures of some NIR phosphorescent Pd(II) complexes (Pd1–Pd30).

The photophysics of luminescent mono-, di- and polynuclear d10 Au(I) complexes has been extensively studied.39,72–74 The most common coordination geometry observed for Au(I) is two-coordinated (linear) or three-coordinated (trigonal planar) resulting in the uniqueness of the weak intermolecular attractive interaction present in between gold centers. Many mononuclear Au(I) complexes containing carbene, phosphine, thiolate and acetylide ligands exhibit RT phosphorescence, originating from 3LMCT or 3ILCT, with relatively lower ΦP compared to Ir(III) and Pt(II) complexes.37 Although it is rare to find mononuclear Au(I) complexes (Au1–Au4)469,470 (Fig. 84) displaying NIR emission (Table 9), for di- and polynuclear Au(I) complexes, the weak intermolecular Au–Au interactions can improve their photophysics and lower the energy of emission bands. For example, [Au2(dppm)2]2+ with a measured Au–Au distance of 2.962 Å exhibits strong orange emission (λem = 570 nm, ΦP = 0.21, τ = 21 μs) at RT.471 The dinuclear Au(I) complexes (Au5–Au7)472 emit NIR emission in solution at RT, however, it probably does not originate from the intermolecular Au–Au interactions but σ(Au–P) → π*(naphthyl) because of the similarity in the emission energies of the 1,8-bis(diphenylphosphino)naphthalene-containing Au7 complex to that of the analogous mononuclear Au4470 complex. It also was revealed that, in the presence of low-lying phosphinonaphthalene ligand π* orbitals, the Au–Au interactions no longer play a significant role in governing the lowest excited-state properties of these complexes. As the luminescence properties of dinuclear Au(I) complexes (Au8 and Au9)473 show a strong dependence on the Au–Au interactions, this unique behaviour can be exploited in the development of sensing materials for K+, as shown in Fig. 85. The emission spectrum of Au8 shows a drop in intensity at ca. 502 nm, with the concomitant formation of a long-lived emission band at ca. 720 nm (τ = 200 ns) upon addition of potassium ion. The cavity size of the benzo-15-crown-5 ring is too small to fit a K+ ion and thus the two Au(I) centers are pushed closely to each other, resulting in some weak Au–Au interactions and subsequently emission change. The low-energy emission band has been proposed to arise from a 3LMMCT (thiolate ligand → Au–Au bond) excited state. There has been an immense research interest devoted to the d10 Cu(I) clusters, such as [Cu4(X)4(L)4] in Section 4.2.2, due to their rich intrinsic photophysical properties. However, photophysical studies related to the d10 Au(I) chalcogenide clusters are relatively rare, possibly due to the relative instability of Au(I) chalcogenides compared to that of gold metal.73 Yam and coworkers have used ancillary diphosphine ligands acting as a “protective coating” to obtain a series of luminescent hexanuclear (Au10),474 decanuclear, and dodecanuclear Au(I) sulfido complexes,73 in which Au10 with the Au–Au distances range from 2.939 to 3.378 Å displays NIR phosphorescence (S → Au–Au 3LMMCT) (λem = 810 nm, τ = 3.6 μs) in solution and red phosphorescence (λem = 630 nm) in solid at RT due to the rigidochromic effects, like [Cu4(X)4(L)4]. The emission band of Au10Se4-cored Au11475 with Au–Au distances of 2.98–3.35 Å is further red shifted at 880 nm in solution and 970 nm in solid at RT (1020 nm in solid at 77 K). It should be noted that, in terms of size, the large polynuclear Au(I) selenide complexes are comparable to small metallic Au clusters or nanoparticles, which are found to show weak NIR emission in the range of 1100–1600 nm.475 An unprecedented Au18-membered thiacrown ether[18]aneS6 thiacrown (Au12)476 directed by Au(I)–Au(I) interactions (3.0145–3.3295 Å) and supported by tridentate phosphine and μ3-sulfido ligands was reported by Yam et al. The sulfides in the central cavity point toward each other and are located on the same plane, allowing them to bind the soft Ag+ ion with immediate and pronounced spectroscopic changes. Reversible binding and spectroscopic changes can be readily achieved upon addition of MeCN without disruption of the Au18 macrocycle (Fig. 86). Fig. 84  Chemical structures of some NIR phosphorescent Au(I) and Au(III) complexes (Au1–Au13) (phenyl rings, hydrogen atoms, and a water molecule inside the cavity have been omitted in Au12 for clarity).

(b) Emission spectral changes of Au12 (1.5 × 10−5 M) and Ag+ (0.26 mM) in MeOH upon addition of MeCN (0–100 μL). (c) Photographs of Au12 (1.5 × 10−5 M) in MeOH under UV irradiation upon addition of (A) 0 M Ag+; (B) <0.75 mM Ag+; (C) 0.75 mM Ag+; (D) 0.75 mM Ag+ and <200 μL MeCN; (E) 0.75 mM Ag+ and 200 μL of MeCN (adapted from ref. 476). 4.9.2 Au(III) complexes. Compared with their isoelectronic Pt(II) complexes, cyclometalated d8 Au(III) complexes that are highly emissive in fluid solutions are still sparse. The high electrophilicity of Au(III) ion renders the unoccupied dσ*(dx2–y2) orbital low-lying and thus the low-energy LMCT excited states close to the emissive intraligand excited states, resulting in effective emission quenching. A representative example is [Au(bpy)(Cl)2]+ which emits in the solid state, but not in solution. To increase the luminescence efficiency of Au(III) complexes, strong-field ligands are desired to push the dσ* orbital to higher-lying energy.73,75 The incorporation of a strong σ-donating auxiliary ligand L, such as mesityl, acetylide or NHC ligands, is found to be one successful way to obtain high ΦP up to 11.4% in solution at RT.477 High-performance yellow OLEDs (EQE = 11.5%) based on [Au(C⁁N⁁C)(CCAr)] complexes are also known.478 Many square-planar d8 complexes are unstable with respect to molecular distortions that lead to a symmetry decrease from D4h to D2d, and this is known to facilitate non-radiative excited state decay, as discussed in Section 4.7.2. A common strategy to avoid this problem is the use of terdentate477,478 or tetradentate chelating ligands rather than bidentate chelating ligands. Unfortunately, contrary to other metalloporphyrins, ordinary Au(III) porphyrin complexes are essentially non-luminescent in solution at RT.75 A recent study reveals that Au(III) N-confused tetraphenylporphyrin complex (Au11)479 (Fig. 84) is emissive in solution at RT. The electron donation from the confused pyrrole to the Au(III) atom through the Csp2–Au(III) bond might contribute to the relatively electron rich Au(III) center, which results in a raising of the energy of the d–d transitions high enough to inhibit emission quenching.The photophysics of luminescent Au(I) complexes has been extensively studied, while, for Au(III) complexes, research until now has focused almost exclusively on C⁁N⁁C and C⁁N⁁N ligands, and there has been essentially no experimental work on other terdentate or tetradentate ligands. Therefore, for either visible or NIR phosphorescence, the photophysics of cyclometalated Au(III) complexes is virtually unexplored.5. Prospects and conclusionsRecent advances in research on NIR phosphorescent materials and applications were described with emphasis on the emerging class of NIR phosphorescent transition-metal complexes and a number of technologically important applications. With respect to material design, various types of transition-metal complexes reported in the literature, such as Cu(I), Au(II), Cr(III), Re(I), Re(III), Ru(II), Os(II), Ir(III), Pt(II), Pd(II), Au(I), and Au(III) complexes, were systemically reviewed, discussed, and compared. Among these complexes, Pt(II) complexes with the 4-coordinate square-planar geometry and strong Pt–Pt interactions have uniquely different and useful photophysical properties in comparison to the other metal ions for the purposes of NIR emission. In general, there are three efficient ways to achieve NIR phosphorescence. The first one is to extend the π-conjugated system in the ligands. The representative and successful examples are Pt(II) π-extended porphyrin complexes, which display many advantages including high thermal and chemical stability, good solubility, multiple options for chemical modification, and high luminescence for a wide variety of applications including NIR OLEDs, PVCs, sensors, TTAUs, and biomedical imaging. The second strategy is to protect Cu(I), Re(III), and Au(I) cluster complexes with ancillary chalcogenide or phosphine ligands. The last tactic involves using intermolecular and/or intramolecular metal–metal interactions to lower emission energy, as discussed in Section 4.7.In respect of the development of NIR phosphorescent transition-metal complexes, the following aspects should be considered. (1) Macromolecules containing NIR phosphorescent transition-metal complexes, especially, dendrimers with the different properties of core and antenna, are expected, due to their many advantages including protection of the long-lived phosphorescent core by functional or biocompatible antenna, good solubility in solvent, convenient fabrication into thin films or nanoparticles, and wide applications. (2) Supermolecules building on self-assembly of transition-metal complexes through intermolecular and/or intramolecular metal–metal interactions, π–π interactions, and/or hydrogen bands might be another hot spot for NIR phosphorescent materials. (3) New chelating ligands, especially porphyrin ligands, with extended π-systems and stronger rigidity are in demand. Previous efforts mainly focused on fusing aromatic units at β-positions of porphyrin, while a recent review480 revealed that porphyrins fused with other aromatic units at both the meso- and β-positions through intramolecular oxidative coupling show very strong bathochromic shifts of absorption (λabs reaching 1.5–2 μm in some cases). This would be helpful for the design of NIR phosphorescent porphyrin complexes. (4) Polynuclear (n > 20) Cu(I), Re(III), and Au(I) cluster complexes protected by ancillary chalcogenide or phosphine ligands are needed because they might have some unique different photophysical properties from small molecules and nano materials. For instance, crown-like Au(I) cluster complexes containing up to 36 Au(I) atoms have been reported.481 In terms of size, these polynuclear Au(I) cluster complexes are comparable to small metallic Au clusters or nanoparticles, which also are found to show weak NIR emission. This might help in the design of luminescent Au nanoparticles with a precise number of metal atoms by using polynuclear Au(I) cluster complexes as the precursors. (5) It has been demonstrated that incorporating DA systems into ligands is an efficient tool for obtaining NIR emission for fluorescent materials,11 while, for NIR phosphorescent transition-metal complexes, there are rare successful examples. Therefore, more attention should be spent on the development of appropriate DA-based transition-metal complexes. (6) To date, the study on NIR phosphorescent transition-metal complexes mainly focus on λem ranging 700 to 1000 nm. Phosphorescent transition-metal complexes with λem longer than 1000 nm are scarce. The longer λem with high ΦP is eagerly expected. Of course, it is not always true that the longer λem is better. Moreover, at any time, it should be kept in mind that the longer λem would lead to the lower ΦP and shorter τ, tentatively rationalized by the low energy gap of which the radiation-less deactivation may be governed by the large amplitude vibration.When this review was prepared, a new study482 demonstrated another strategy for obtaining long-lived triplet excited state by excited states intramolecular proton transfer (ESIPT) in a series of naphthalimides conjugated with 2-(2-hydroxyphenyl) benzothiazole (Fig. 87). For these purely organic chromophores, the absence of heavy-atom SOC effects leads to no phosphorescence but strong red-shifted fluorescence at RT. However, very long-lived triplet excited states up to 124.5 μs are observed by transient absorption analysis. It is interesting that these long-lived triplet excited states are also sensitive to oxygen, which might be a new direction for the design of oxygen probes and will inspire more investigations into this fascinating field in the use of long-lived triplet excited states (beyond phosphorescence). Table Content:
Haifeng Xiang	Haifeng Xiang obtained his PhD from the Department of Chemistry and Department of Electrical & Electronic Engineering, The University of Hong Kong, Hong Kong, P. R. China in 2005 under the supervision of Prof. C. M. Che and P. T. Lai. After a post-doctoral stay in the same group, he joined the College of Chemistry, Sichuan University, P. R. China, as an Associate Professor in 2009. His research interests include fluorescent/phosphorescent probes, phosphorescent Pt(II) complexes, and organic optoelectronic materials.
Jinghui Cheng	Jinghui Cheng is currently a MS student of the College of Chemistry, Sichuan University, P. R. China. She received her BS degree from Zhoukou Normal University, P. R. China, in 2011. Her research interests involve phosphorescent Pt(II) complexes.
Xiaofeng Ma	Xiaofeng Ma is currently a MS student of the College of Chemistry, Sichuan University, P. R. China. She received her BS degree from Datong University, P. R. China, in 2012. Her research interests involve phosphorescent Pt(II) complexes.
Xiangge Zhou	Xiangge Zhou obtained his PhD from the Department of Chemistry, The University of Hong Kong, Hong Kong, P. R. China in 1999. After a post-doctoral stay at the Technische Universität München, German, he joined the College of Chemistry, Sichuan University, P. R. China, as a Professor in 2003. His research interests include synthesis and catalysis of organometallic compounds and asymmetric synthesis.
Jason Joseph Chruma	Jason Joseph Chruma obtained his PhD from the Department of Chemistry, University of Pennsylvania, USA in 2002 under the supervision of Prof. Amos B. Smith, III. After an NIH post-doctoral fellowship at Columbia University, USA with Prof. Ronald C. Breslow, he joined the Department of Chemistry, University of Virginia, USA, as an Assistant Professor in 2005. In 2012, he transitioned to the College of Chemistry, Sichuan University, P. R. China, as a Professor. His research interests include the development of natural product-based electrophiles as fluorescent linchpin reagents in bioconjugate synthesis.
